(2&#160;weeks and 3&#160;days)</td>

<th scope="row">Location</th>
<td><span class="location"><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Flag_of_Egypt.svg/23px-Flag_of_Egypt.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Flag_of_Egypt.svg/35px-Flag_of_Egypt.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Flag_of_Egypt.svg/45px-Flag_of_Egypt.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" />&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a></span><br />

<th scope="row">Causes</th>
<td>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Police_brutality" title="Police brutality">Police brutality</a><sup id="cite_ref-AFP-egyptbraces_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AFP-egyptbraces-1">[1]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Emergency_law_in_Egypt" title="Emergency law in Egypt">State-of-emergency laws</a><sup id="cite_ref-AFP-egyptbraces_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AFP-egyptbraces-1">[1]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Electoral_fraud" title="Electoral fraud">Electoral fraud</a></li>
<li>Political censorship<sup id="cite_ref-alarabiya-plans_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alarabiya-plans-2">[2]</a></sup></li>
<li>Corruption<sup id="cite_ref-alarabiya-plans_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alarabiya-plans-2">[2]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">Unemployment</a><sup id="cite_ref-tna_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tna-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">Food price rises</a><sup id="cite_ref-tna_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tna-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Minimum_wage" title="Minimum wage">Low wages</a><sup id="cite_ref-AFP-egyptbraces_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AFP-egyptbraces-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tna_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tna-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cliodynamics" title="Cliodynamics">Demographic structural factors</a><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Other regional protests</a></li>
</ul>
</td>
</tr>

</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Result</th>
<td>
<ul>
<li>Overthrow of <a href="/wiki/Mubarak_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Mubarak era">Mubarak government</a></li>
<li>Resignation of Prime Ministers <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Nazif" title="Ahmed Nazif">Nazif</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Shafik" title="Ahmed Shafik">Shafik</a><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></li>
<li>Assumption of power by the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Council_of_the_Armed_Forces" title="Supreme Council of the Armed Forces">military</a><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></li>
<li>Suspension of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Constitution_of_1971" title="Egyptian Constitution of 1971">Constitution</a> and dissolution of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_parliamentary_election,_2010" title="Egyptian parliamentary election, 2010">Parliament</a><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></li>
<li>Disbanding of the <a href="/wiki/State_Security_Investigations_Service" title="State Security Investigations Service">State Security Investigations Service</a><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></li>
<li>Dissolution of the <a href="/wiki/National_Democratic_Party_(Egypt)" title="National Democratic Party (Egypt)">NDP</a> (former ruling party) and transfer of its assets to the state<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></li>
<li>Prosecution of Mubarak, his family and former ministers<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-reutersmurdercharges_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutersmurdercharges-12">[12]</a></sup></li>
<li>31-year <a href="/wiki/Emergency_law_in_Egypt" title="Emergency law in Egypt">state of emergency</a> lifted<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Egyptian_presidential_election,_2012" title="Egyptian presidential election, 2012">Democratic election</a> to replace Mubarak; <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Morsi" title="Mohamed Morsi">Mohamed Morsi</a> elected as new president<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/2012%E2%80%9313_Egyptian_protests" title="2012–13 Egyptian protests">Protests</a> in response to Morsi's temporary constitutional declaration</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/June_2013_Egyptian_protests" title="June 2013 Egyptian protests">Protests</a> marking the one-year anniversary of Morsi's inauguration as president followed by a <a href="/wiki/2013_Egyptian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="2013 Egyptian coup d'état">coup d'état</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Post-coup_unrest_in_Egypt_(2013%E2%80%9314)" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-coup unrest in Egypt (2013–14)">Civil unrest</a> in response to coup</li>
<li>Another <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_presidential_election,_2014" title="Egyptian presidential election, 2014">presidential election</a> held in 2014; Egyptian defense minister Gen. <a href="/wiki/Abdel_Fattah_el-Sisi" title="Abdel Fattah el-Sisi">Abdel Fattah el-Sisi</a> elected as president</li>
<li>Morsi and other Muslim Brotherhood figures <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment_in_Egypt" title="Capital punishment in Egypt">sentenced to death</a> on various charges</li>
</ul>
</td>
</tr>

<i>See: <a href="#Cities_and_regions:_mass_civil_disobedience">Regions</a> section below.</i></td>

<li><b><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_2011_Egyptian_revolution_under_Supreme_Council_of_the_Armed_Forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Timeline of the 2011 Egyptian revolution under Supreme Council of the Armed Forces">Post-Revolution</a>:</b> 300+<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>

<td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;border-top:none;font-size:smaller;"><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt"><img alt="Egypt" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Flag_of_Egypt.svg/23px-Flag_of_Egypt.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Flag_of_Egypt.svg/35px-Flag_of_Egypt.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Flag_of_Egypt.svg/45px-Flag_of_Egypt.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a></span></td>

<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Egyptian_crisis_(2011%E2%80%9314)" title="Template:Egyptian crisis (2011–14)"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Egyptian_crisis_(2011%E2%80%9314)" title="Template talk:Egyptian crisis (2011–14)"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Egyptian_crisis_(2011%E2%80%9314)&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>

<p>The <b>Egyptian revolution of 2011</b>, locally known as the <b>January 25 Revolution</b> (<a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Egyptian Arabic language">Egyptian Arabic</a>: <span lang="arz" dir="rtl">ثورة 25 يناير</span>‎‎; <span title="Arabic transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none"><i>Thawret 25 yanāyir</i></span>),<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> began on 25 January 2011 and took place across all of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. It consisted of demonstrations, marches, occupations of plazas, riots, non-violent <a href="/wiki/Civil_resistance" title="Civil resistance">civil resistance</a>, acts of <a href="/wiki/Civil_disobedience" title="Civil disobedience">civil disobedience</a> and strikes. Millions of protesters from a range of <a href="/wiki/Socio-economic" class="mw-redirect" title="Socio-economic">socio-economic</a> and religious backgrounds demanded the overthrow of <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_President" class="mw-redirect" title="Egyptian President">Egyptian President</a> <a href="/wiki/Hosni_Mubarak" title="Hosni Mubarak">Hosni Mubarak</a>. The revolution included Islamic, liberal, anti-capitalist, nationalist and feminist elements. Violent clashes between security forces and protesters resulted in at least 846 people killed and over 6,000 injured.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Protesters burned over 90 police stations.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The protests took place in <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> and other cities.</p>
<p>The Egyptian protesters' grievances focused on legal and political issues,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Police_brutality" title="Police brutality">police brutality</a>, state-of-emergency laws,<sup id="cite_ref-AFP-egyptbraces_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AFP-egyptbraces-1">[1]</a></sup> lack of free elections and <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">freedom of speech</a>, corruption,<sup id="cite_ref-alarabiya-plans_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alarabiya-plans-2">[2]</a></sup> and economic issues including high unemployment, food-price inflation<sup id="cite_ref-tna_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tna-3">[3]</a></sup> and low wages.<sup id="cite_ref-AFP-egyptbraces_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AFP-egyptbraces-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tna_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tna-3">[3]</a></sup> The protesters' primary demands were the end of the Mubarak regime and emergency law, freedom, justice, a responsive non-military government and a voice in managing Egypt's resources.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Strikes by <a href="/wiki/Trade_unions_in_Egypt" title="Trade unions in Egypt">labour unions</a> added to the pressure on government officials.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the uprising the capital, Cairo, was described as "a war zone"<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> and the port city of <a href="/wiki/Suez" title="Suez">Suez</a> saw frequent violent clashes. Protesters defied a government-imposed <a href="/wiki/Curfew" title="Curfew">curfew</a>, which was unenforced by the police and military. Egypt's <a href="/wiki/Central_Security_Forces" title="Central Security Forces">Central Security Forces</a> police, loyal to Mubarak, was gradually replaced by military troops. In the chaos, there was <a href="/wiki/Looting" title="Looting">looting</a> by gangs which was instigated (according to opposition sources) by plainclothes police officers. In response, watch groups were organized by civilians to protect neighbourhoods.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>International reaction has varied, with most Western nations condoning peaceful protests but concerned about the stability of Egypt and the region. The Egyptian and Tunisian revolutions have influenced demonstrations in other Arab countries, including <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>.</p>
<p>Mubarak dissolved his government, appointing former head of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_General_Intelligence_Directorate" class="mw-redirect" title="Egyptian General Intelligence Directorate">Egyptian General Intelligence Directorate</a> <a href="/wiki/Omar_Suleiman" title="Omar Suleiman">Omar Suleiman</a> vice-president in an attempt to quell dissent. Mubarak asked aviation minister and former chief of Egypt's air force <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Shafik" title="Ahmed Shafik">Ahmed Shafik</a> to form a new government. <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_ElBaradei" title="Mohamed ElBaradei">Mohamed ElBaradei</a> became a major opposition figure, with all major opposition groups supporting his role as negotiator for a transitional unity government.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> In response to mounting pressure, Mubarak announced he did not intend to seek re-election in September.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 11 February 2011 Vice President Omar Suleiman announced that Mubarak would resign as president, turning power over to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Council_of_the_Armed_Forces" title="Supreme Council of the Armed Forces">Supreme Council of the Armed Forces</a> (SCAF).<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The military junta, headed by effective head of state <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Hussein_Tantawi" title="Mohamed Hussein Tantawi">Mohamed Hussein Tantawi</a>, announced on 13 February that the constitution would be suspended, both houses of parliament dissolved and the military would rule for six months (until elections could be held). The previous cabinet, including Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Shafik" title="Ahmed Shafik">Ahmed Shafik</a>, would serve as a caretaker government until a new one was formed.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Shafik resigned on 3 March, a day before major protests to force him to step down were planned, and was replaced by former transport minister <a href="/wiki/Essam_Sharaf" title="Essam Sharaf">Essam Sharaf</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> On 24 May 2011, Mubarak was ordered to stand trial on charges of <a href="/wiki/Premeditated_murder" class="mw-redirect" title="Premeditated murder">premeditated murder</a> of peaceful protesters and, if convicted, could face the death penalty.<sup id="cite_ref-reutersmurdercharges_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutersmurdercharges-12">[12]</a></sup> On 2 June 2012 Mubarak was found guilty of complicity in the murder of protesters and sentenced to life imprisonment, but the sentence was overturned on appeal and a retrial ordered.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> A number of protesters, upset that others tried with Mubarak (including his two sons) were acquitted, took to the streets.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Mubarak was eventually cleared of all charges on 29 November 2014,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> although Egypt's prosecutor general announced he would appeal the verdict.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the revolution against Mubarak and a period of rule by the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Council_of_the_Armed_Forces" title="Supreme Council of the Armed Forces">Supreme Council of the Armed Forces</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_Brotherhood" title="Muslim Brotherhood">Muslim Brotherhood</a> took power in Egypt through a series of popular elections, with Egyptians electing Islamist <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Morsi" title="Mohamed Morsi">Mohamed Morsi</a> to the presidency in June 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> However, Morsi's government encountered fierce opposition from secularists and members of the military, and <a href="/wiki/June_2013_Egyptian_protests" title="June 2013 Egyptian protests">mass protests</a> broke out against his rule in June 2013. On 3 July 2013, Morsi was deposed by a <a href="/wiki/2013_Egyptian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="2013 Egyptian coup d'état">coup d'état</a> led by the minister of defense, <a href="/wiki/Abdel_Fattah_el-Sisi" title="Abdel Fattah el-Sisi">General Abdel Fattah El-Sisi</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> who became Egypt's <i>de facto</i> strongman and was eventually elected president himself in a <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_presidential_election,_2014" title="Egyptian presidential election, 2014">2014 election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_names"><span id="Naming"></span>Other names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Other names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In Egypt and other parts of the Arab world, the protests and governmental changes are also known as the <em>25 January Revolution</em> (<span lang="ar">ثورة 25 يناير</span> <i>Thawrat 25 Yanāyir</i>), <em>Freedom Revolution</em> (<span lang="ar">ثورة حرية</span> <i>Thawrat Horeya</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> or <em>Rage Revolution</em> (<span lang="ar">ثورة الغضب</span> <i>Thawrat al-Ġaḍab</i>), and (less frequently)<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> the <em>Youth Revolution</em> (<span lang="ar">ثورة الشباب</span> <i>Thawrat al-Shabāb</i>), <em><a href="/wiki/Nelumbo" title="Nelumbo">Lotus</a> Revolution</em><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> (<span lang="ar">ثورة اللوتس</span>) or <em>White Revolution</em> (<span lang="ar">الثورة البيضاء</span> <i>al-Thawrah al-bayḍāʾ</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hosni_Mubarak_ritratto.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Smiling man in suit and tie" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Hosni_Mubarak_ritratto.jpg/170px-Hosni_Mubarak_ritratto.jpg" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Hosni_Mubarak_ritratto.jpg/255px-Hosni_Mubarak_ritratto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Hosni_Mubarak_ritratto.jpg/340px-Hosni_Mubarak_ritratto.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="1000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hosni_Mubarak_ritratto.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Hosni_Mubarak" title="Hosni Mubarak">Hosni Mubarak</a> in 2009</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hosni_Mubarak" title="Hosni Mubarak">Hosni Mubarak</a> became <a href="/wiki/President_of_Egypt" title="President of Egypt">President of Egypt</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Anwar_El_Sadat" class="mw-redirect" title="Assassination of Anwar El Sadat">assassination of Anwar El Sadat</a> in 1981. Mubarak's <a href="/wiki/National_Democratic_Party_(Egypt)" title="National Democratic Party (Egypt)">National Democratic Party (NDS)</a> maintained one-party rule under a continual state of emergency.<sup id="cite_ref-Hosni_Mubarak_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hosni_Mubarak-50">[50]</a></sup> His government received support from the West and aid from the United States by its suppression of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_militants" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic militants">Islamic militants</a> and peace with <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hosni_Mubarak_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hosni_Mubarak-50">[50]</a></sup> Mubarak was often compared to an Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaoh</a> by the media and some critics, due to his authoritarian rule.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> He was in the 30th year of his reign when the Revolution of 2011 began.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inheritance_of_power">Inheritance of power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Inheritance of power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gamal_Mubarak.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Man talking into a microphone at the World Economic Forum" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Gamal_Mubarak.jpg/170px-Gamal_Mubarak.jpg" width="170" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Gamal_Mubarak.jpg/255px-Gamal_Mubarak.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Gamal_Mubarak.jpg/340px-Gamal_Mubarak.jpg 2x" data-file-width="587" data-file-height="651" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gamal_Mubarak.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Gamal_Mubarak" title="Gamal Mubarak">Gamal Mubarak</a> in 2006</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Mubarak" title="Gamal Mubarak">Gamal Mubarak</a></div>
<p>Gamal Mubarak, Mubarak's younger son, was expected to succeed his father as the next president of Egypt in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Gamal began receiving attention from the Egyptian media, since there were apparently no other heirs to the presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bashar_al-Assad" title="Bashar al-Assad">Bashar al-Assad</a>'s rise to power in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> in June 2000, hours after <a href="/wiki/Hafez_al-Assad" title="Hafez al-Assad">Hafez al-Assad</a>'s death, sparked debate in the Egyptian press about the prospects for a similar scenario in Cairo.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the years after <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_presidential_election,_2005" title="Egyptian presidential election, 2005">Mubarak's 2005 re-election</a>, several left- and right-wing (primarily unofficial) political groups expressed opposition to the inheritance of power, demanded reforms and asked for a multi-candidate election. In 2006, with opposition increasing, <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_News_Egypt" title="Daily News Egypt">Daily News Egypt</a></i> reported an online campaign initiative (the National Initiative against Power Inheritance) demanding that Gamal reduce his power. The campaign said, "President Mubarak and his son constantly denied even the possibility of [succession]. However, in reality they did the opposite, including amending the constitution to make sure that Gamal will be the only unchallenged candidate."<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the decade, public perception grew that Gamal would succeed his father. He wielded increasing power as NDP deputy secretary general and chair of the party's policy committee. Analysts described Mubarak's last decade in power as "the age of Gamal Mubarak". With his father’s health declining and no appointed vice-president, Gamal was considered Egypt's <i>de facto</i> president by some.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Although Gamal and Hosni Mubarak denied an inheritance of power, Gamal could be elected; with Hosni Mubarak's presidential term set to expire in 2010, speculation existed that Gamal would run as the NDP candidate in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> However, after the January–February 2011 protest Gamal Mubarak said that he would not run for president in the 2011 elections.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergency_law">Emergency law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Emergency law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Emergency_law_in_Egypt" title="Emergency law in Egypt">Emergency law in Egypt</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Emergency_law_in_Egypt" title="Emergency law in Egypt">Emergency law</a> (Law No. 162 of 1958) was enacted in the country after the 1967 <a href="/wiki/Six-Day_War" title="Six-Day War">Six-Day War</a>. Although it was suspended for 18 months during the early 1980s,<sup id="cite_ref-emerlaw_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emerlaw-60">[60]</a></sup> it has otherwise continuously been in effect since <a href="/wiki/Anwar_Sadat" title="Anwar Sadat">Anwar Sadat</a>'s 1981 assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-eohr2008_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eohr2008-61">[61]</a></sup> Emergency law extended police powers, suspended <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Egypt" title="Constitution of Egypt">constitutional rights</a>, legalised censorship<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> and abolished <i><a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">habeas corpus</a></i>. It limits non-governmental political activity, including <a href="/wiki/Demonstration_(people)" class="mw-redirect" title="Demonstration (people)">demonstrations</a>, unapproved political organizations and unregistered financial donations.<sup id="cite_ref-emerlaw_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emerlaw-60">[60]</a></sup> The Mubarak government has cited the threat of terrorism in extending emergency law,<sup id="cite_ref-eohr2008_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eohr2008-61">[61]</a></sup> claiming that opposition groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_Brotherhood_in_Egypt" title="Muslim Brotherhood in Egypt">Muslim Brotherhood</a> could gain power in Egypt if the government did not forgo <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_elections" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliamentary elections">parliamentary elections</a> and suppress the group through emergency law.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> This has led to the imprisonment of activists without trial,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> illegal, undocumented and hidden detention facilities<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> and the rejection of university and love, mosque and newspaper staff based on their political affiliation.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_parliamentary_election,_2010" title="Egyptian parliamentary election, 2010">December 2010 parliamentary election</a> was preceded by a media crackdown, arrests, candidate bans (particularly Muslim Brotherhood candidates) and allegations of fraud due to the near-unanimous victory by the NDP in parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-emerlaw_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emerlaw-60">[60]</a></sup> Human-rights organizations estimate that in 2010, between 5,000 and 10,000 people were in long-term detention without charge or trial.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Police_brutality">Police brutality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Police brutality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_in_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Law enforcement in Egypt">Law enforcement in Egypt</a></div>
<p>According to a U.S. Embassy report, police brutality has been widespread in Egypt.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> In the five years before the revolution, the Mubarak regime denied the existence of torture or abuse by police. However, claims by domestic and international groups provided cellphone videos or first-hand accounts of hundreds of cases of police brutality.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> According to the 2009 Human Rights Report from the U.S. State Department, "Domestic and international human rights groups reported that the Ministry of Interior (MOI) State Security Investigative Service (SSIS), police, and other government entities continued to employ torture to extract information or force confessions. The Egyptian Organization for Human Rights documented 30 cases of torture during the year 2009. In numerous trials defendants alleged that police tortured them during questioning. During the year activists and observers circulated some amateur cellphone videos documenting the alleged abuse of citizens by security officials. For example, on 8 February, a blogger posted a video of two police officers, identified by their first names and last initials, sodomizing a bound naked man named Ahmed Abdel Fattah Ali with a bottle. On 12 August, the same blogger posted two videos of alleged police torture of a man in a Port Said police station by the head of investigations, Mohammed Abu Ghazala. There was no indication that the government investigated either case."<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>The deployment of <a href="/wiki/Baltagiya" title="Baltagiya">Baltageya</a><sup id="cite_ref-Gunfire_Rings_Out_as_Protesters_Clash_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunfire_Rings_Out_as_Protesters_Clash-72">[72]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">بلطجية</span>‎‎)—plainclothes police—by the NDP has been a hallmark of the Mubarak government.<sup id="cite_ref-Gunfire_Rings_Out_as_Protesters_Clash_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunfire_Rings_Out_as_Protesters_Clash-72">[72]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Organisation_for_Human_Rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Egyptian Organisation for Human Rights">Egyptian Organisation for Human Rights</a> has documented 567 cases of torture, including 167 deaths, by police from 1993 to 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Excessive force was often used by law-enforcement agencies against popular uprisings.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> On 6 June 2010 <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Khaled_Mohamed_Saeed" title="Death of Khaled Mohamed Saeed">Khaled Mohamed Saeed died under disputed circumstances</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sidi_Gaber" title="Sidi Gaber">Sidi Gaber</a> area of <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, with witnesses testifying that he was beaten to death by police - an event which galvanized Egyptians around the issue of police brutality.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> A Facebook page, "We are all Khaled Said", helped attract nationwide attention to the case.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_ElBaradei" title="Mohamed ElBaradei">Mohamed ElBaradei</a>, former head of the <a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">International Atomic Energy Agency</a>, led a 2010 rally in Alexandria against police abuse, and visited Saeed's family to offer condolences.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the January–February 2011 protests, police brutality was common. Jack Shenker, a reporter for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i>, was arrested during the Cairo protests on 26 January. He witnessed fellow Egyptian protesters being tortured, assaulted, and taken to undisclosed locations by police officers. Shenker and other detainees were released after covert intervention by <a href="/wiki/Ayman_Nour" title="Ayman Nour">Ayman Nour</a>, the father of a fellow detainee.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_corruption"><span id="Corruption_in_government_elections"></span>Election corruption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Election corruption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Corruption, coercion not to vote and manipulation of election results occurred during many elections over Mubarak's 30-year rule.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Until 2005, Mubarak was the only presidential candidate (with a yes-or-no vote).<sup id="cite_ref-finalreport_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-finalreport-84">[84]</a></sup> Mubarak won five consecutive presidential elections with a sweeping majority. Although opposition groups and international election-monitoring agencies charged that the elections were rigged, those agencies were not allowed to monitor elections. The only opposition presidential candidate in recent Egyptian history, Ayman Nour, was imprisoned before the 2005 elections.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> According to a 2007 UN survey, voter turnout was extremely low (about 25 percent) because of a lack of trust in the political system.<sup id="cite_ref-finalreport_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-finalreport-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographic_and_economic_challenges">Demographic and economic challenges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Demographic and economic challenges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Unemployment_and_reliance_on_subsidized_goods">Unemployment and reliance on subsidized goods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Unemployment and reliance on subsidized goods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Egypt" title="Demographics of Egypt">Demographics of Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Demographic_trap" title="Demographic trap">Demographic trap</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Youth_bulge" class="mw-redirect" title="Youth bulge">Youth bulge</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Egypt_population_pyramid_2005.svg" class="image"><img alt="Pyramid graph, divided by age and gender" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Egypt_population_pyramid_2005.svg/220px-Egypt_population_pyramid_2005.svg.png" width="220" height="119" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Egypt_population_pyramid_2005.svg/330px-Egypt_population_pyramid_2005.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Egypt_population_pyramid_2005.svg/440px-Egypt_population_pyramid_2005.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="479" data-file-height="260" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Egypt_population_pyramid_2005.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Population_pyramid" title="Population pyramid">population pyramid</a> in 2005; many people age 30 and younger, despite education, have difficulty finding work.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The population of Egypt grew from 30,083,419 in 1966<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> to roughly 79,000,000 by 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> The vast majority of Egyptians live near the banks of the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a>, in an area of about 40,000 square kilometers (15,000&#160;sq&#160;mi) where the only <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable land</a> is found. In late 2010, about 40 percent of Egypt's population lived on the equivalent of roughly USD$2 per day, with a large portion relying on subsidized goods.<sup id="cite_ref-AFP-egyptbraces_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AFP-egyptbraces-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Peterson_Institute_for_International_Economics" title="Peterson Institute for International Economics">Peterson Institute for International Economics</a> and other proponents of demographic structural approach (<a href="/wiki/Cliodynamics" title="Cliodynamics">cliodynamics</a>), a basic problem in Egypt is unemployment driven by a demographic <a href="/wiki/Youth_bulge" class="mw-redirect" title="Youth bulge">youth bulge</a>; with the number of new people entering the workforce at about four percent a year, unemployment in Egypt is almost 10 times as high for <a href="/wiki/College_graduate" class="mw-redirect" title="College graduate">college graduates</a> as for those who finished elementary school (particularly educated urban youth—the people who were in the streets during the revolution).<sup id="cite_ref-cliodynamics.ru_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cliodynamics.ru-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Daveness_98_-_Cityscape_in_Cairo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Tenements with satellite dishes and debris" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Flickr_-_Daveness_98_-_Cityscape_in_Cairo.jpg/220px-Flickr_-_Daveness_98_-_Cityscape_in_Cairo.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Flickr_-_Daveness_98_-_Cityscape_in_Cairo.jpg/330px-Flickr_-_Daveness_98_-_Cityscape_in_Cairo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Flickr_-_Daveness_98_-_Cityscape_in_Cairo.jpg/440px-Flickr_-_Daveness_98_-_Cityscape_in_Cairo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Daveness_98_-_Cityscape_in_Cairo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A poor neighbourhood in Cairo</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy_and_poor_living_conditions"><span id="Poor_living_conditions_and_economic_conditions"></span>Economy and poor living conditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Economy and poor living conditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Egypt" title="Economy of Egypt">Economy of Egypt</a></div>
<p>Egypt's economy was highly centralised during the presidency of <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a>, becoming more <a href="/wiki/Market_economy" title="Market economy">market-driven</a> under Anwar Sadat and Mubarak. From 2004 to 2008 the Mubarak government pursued economic reform to attract foreign investment and increase GDP, later postponing further reforms because of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">Great Recession</a>. The international <a href="/wiki/Economic_downturn" class="mw-redirect" title="Economic downturn">economic downturn</a> slowed Egypt's GDP growth to 4.5 percent in 2009. In 2010, analysts said that the government of Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Nazif" title="Ahmed Nazif">Ahmed Nazif</a> would need to resume economic reform to attract foreign investment, increase growth and improve economic conditions. Despite recent high national economic growth, living conditions for the average Egyptian remained relatively poor<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> (albeit better than other African nations<sup id="cite_ref-cliodynamics.ru_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cliodynamics.ru-88">[88]</a></sup> with no significant social upheavals).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Corruption"><span id="Corruption_among_government_officials"></span>Corruption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Corruption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Crime_in_Egypt" title="Crime in Egypt">Crime in Egypt</a></div>
<p>Political corruption in the Mubarak administration's <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Interior_(Egypt)" title="Ministry of Interior (Egypt)">Interior Ministry</a> rose dramatically, due to increased control of the system necessary to sustain his presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The rise to power of powerful businessmen in the NDP, the government and the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Representatives_(Egypt)" title="House of Representatives (Egypt)">House of Representatives</a> led to public anger during the Ahmed Nazif government. <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Ezz_(businessman)" title="Ahmed Ezz (businessman)">Ahmed Ezz</a> <a href="/wiki/Monopoly" title="Monopoly">monopolised</a> the steel industry, with more than 60 percent of market share.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Aladdin Elaasar, an Egyptian biographer and American professor, estimated that the Mubarak family was worth from $50&#160;to $70&#160;billion.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>The wealth of former NDP secretary Ezz was estimated at 18&#160;billion <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_pound" title="Egyptian pound">Egyptian pounds</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Obama_optimistic_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obama_optimistic-95">[95]</a></sup> the wealth of former housing minister Ahmed al-Maghraby was estimated at more than 11&#160;billion Egyptian pounds;<sup id="cite_ref-Obama_optimistic_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obama_optimistic-95">[95]</a></sup> that of former tourism minister <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zuhair_Garrana&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Zuhair Garrana (page does not exist)">Zuhair Garrana</a> is estimated at 13&#160;billion Egyptian pounds;<sup id="cite_ref-Obama_optimistic_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obama_optimistic-95">[95]</a></sup> former minister of trade and industry Rashid Mohamed Rashid is estimated to be worth 12&#160;billion Egyptian pounds,<sup id="cite_ref-Obama_optimistic_95-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obama_optimistic-95">[95]</a></sup> and former interior minister Habib al-Adly was estimated to be worth eight billion Egyptian pounds.<sup id="cite_ref-Obama_optimistic_95-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obama_optimistic-95">[95]</a></sup> The perception among Egyptians was that the only people benefiting from the nation's wealth were businessmen with ties to the National Democratic Party: "Wealth fuels political power and political power buys wealth."<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_parliamentary_election,_2010" title="Egyptian parliamentary election, 2010">2010 elections</a>, opposition groups complained about government harassment and fraud. Opposition and citizen activists called for changes to <a href="/wiki/National_Association_for_Change#Platform" title="National Association for Change">a number of legal</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Association_for_Change#Goals" title="National Association for Change">constitutional provisions affecting elections</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In 2010, <a href="/wiki/Transparency_International" title="Transparency International">Transparency International</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Corruption_Perceptions_Index" title="Corruption Perceptions Index">Corruption Perceptions Index</a> (CPI) gave Egypt a score of 3.1 based on perceptions by business people and analysts of the degree of corruption (with 10 being clean, and 0 totally corrupt).<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prelude"><span id="Lead-up_to_the_protests"></span>Prelude</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Prelude">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>To prepare for the possible overthrow of Mubarak, opposition groups studied <a href="/wiki/Gene_Sharp" title="Gene Sharp">Gene Sharp</a>'s work on nonviolent action and worked with leaders of <a href="/wiki/Otpor!" title="Otpor!">Otpor!</a>, the student-led <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbian</a> <a href="/wiki/Overthrow_of_Slobodan_Milo%C5%A1evi%C4%87" title="Overthrow of Slobodan Milošević">organisation</a>. Copies of Sharp's list of 198 non-violent "weapons", translated into Arabic and not always attributed to him, were circulated in <a href="/wiki/Tahrir_Square" title="Tahrir Square">Tahrir Square</a> during its occupation.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tunisian_revolution">Tunisian revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Tunisian revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tunisian_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Tunisian revolution">Tunisian revolution</a></div>
<p>Following the ousting of Tunisian president Zine El Abidine Ben Ali after mass protests, many analysts (including former <a href="/wiki/European_Commission_President" class="mw-redirect" title="European Commission President">European Commission President</a> <a href="/wiki/Romano_Prodi" title="Romano Prodi">Romano Prodi</a>) saw Egypt as the next country where such a revolution might occur.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> According to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i>, "The Jasmine Revolution [...] should serve as a stark warning to Arab leaders – beginning with Egypt's 83-year-old Hosni Mubarak – that their refusal to allow more economic and political opportunity is dangerous and untenable."<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Others believed that Egypt was not ready for revolution, citing little aspiration by the Egyptian people, low educational levels and a strong government with military support.<sup id="cite_ref-TimeNoRev_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TimeNoRev-102">[102]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> said, "The simple fact is that most Egyptians do not see any way that they can change their country or their lives through political action, be it voting, activism, or going out on the streets to demonstrate."<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-immolation">Self-immolation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Self-immolation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_lion_of_Egyptian_revolution_(Qasr_al-Nil_Bridge)-edit2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Man crouched on top of a large stone lion, waving a red, white and blue flag" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/The_lion_of_Egyptian_revolution_%28Qasr_al-Nil_Bridge%29-edit2.jpg/220px-The_lion_of_Egyptian_revolution_%28Qasr_al-Nil_Bridge%29-edit2.jpg" width="220" height="284" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/The_lion_of_Egyptian_revolution_%28Qasr_al-Nil_Bridge%29-edit2.jpg/330px-The_lion_of_Egyptian_revolution_%28Qasr_al-Nil_Bridge%29-edit2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/The_lion_of_Egyptian_revolution_%28Qasr_al-Nil_Bridge%29-edit2.jpg/440px-The_lion_of_Egyptian_revolution_%28Qasr_al-Nil_Bridge%29-edit2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1557" data-file-height="2013" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_lion_of_Egyptian_revolution_(Qasr_al-Nil_Bridge)-edit2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Protester holds Egyptian flag during protests which began on 25 January 2011.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Self-immolation" title="Self-immolation">self-immolation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Bouazizi" title="Mohamed Bouazizi">Mohamed Bouazizi</a> in Tunisia on 17 December, a man set himself afire on 18 January in front of the Egyptian parliament<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> and five more attempts followed.<sup id="cite_ref-TimeNoRev_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TimeNoRev-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_Police_Day_protests">National Police Day protests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: National Police Day protests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Opposition groups planned a day of revolt for 25 January, coinciding with <a href="/wiki/National_Police_Day_(Egypt)" title="National Police Day (Egypt)">National Police Day</a>, to protest police brutality in front of the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Interior_(Egypt)" title="Ministry of Interior (Egypt)">Ministry of Interior</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Protesters also demanded the resignation of the Minister of Interior, an end to State corruption, the end of emergency law and presidential <a href="/wiki/Term_limit" title="Term limit">term limits</a> for the president.</p>
<p>Many political movements, opposition parties and public figures supported the day of revolt, including Youth for Justice and Freedom, the <a href="/wiki/Coalition_of_the_Youth_of_the_Revolution" title="Coalition of the Youth of the Revolution">Coalition of the Youth of the Revolution</a>, the Popular Democratic Movement for Change, the <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_Socialists" class="mw-redirect" title="Revolutionary Socialists">Revolutionary Socialists</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Association_for_Change" title="National Association for Change">National Association for Change</a>. The <a href="/wiki/April_6_Youth_Movement" title="April 6 Youth Movement">April 6 Youth Movement</a> was a major supporter of the protest, distributing 20,000 leaflets saying "I will protest on 25 January for my rights". The <a href="/wiki/Ghad_El-Thawra_Party" title="Ghad El-Thawra Party">Ghad El-Thawra Party</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dignity_Party_(Egypt)" title="Dignity Party (Egypt)">Karama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wafd_party" class="mw-redirect" title="Wafd party">Wafd</a> and <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Front_Party" title="Democratic Front Party">Democratic Front</a> supported the protests. The Muslim Brotherhood, Egypt's largest opposition group,<sup id="cite_ref-Tagammu_and_Coptic_Church_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tagammu_and_Coptic_Church-106">[106]</a></sup> confirmed on 23 January that it would participate.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Public figures, including novelist <a href="/wiki/Alaa_Al_Aswany" title="Alaa Al Aswany">Alaa Al Aswany</a>, writer <a href="/wiki/Belal_Fadl" title="Belal Fadl">Belal Fadl</a> and actors <a href="/wiki/Amr_Waked" title="Amr Waked">Amr Waked</a> and <a href="/wiki/Khaled_Aboul_Naga" class="mw-redirect" title="Khaled Aboul Naga">Khaled Aboul Naga</a>, announced that they would participate. The <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics" title="Left-wing politics">leftist</a> <a href="/wiki/National_Progressive_Unionist_Party" title="National Progressive Unionist Party">National Progressive Unionist Party</a> (the <i>Tagammu</i>) said that it would not participate, and the Coptic Church urged Christians not to participate in the protests.<sup id="cite_ref-Tagammu_and_Coptic_Church_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tagammu_and_Coptic_Church-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>Twenty-six-year-old <a href="/wiki/Asmaa_Mahfouz" title="Asmaa Mahfouz">Asmaa Mahfouz</a> was instrumental<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> in sparking the protests.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> In a video blog posted a week before National Police Day,<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> she urged the Egyptian people to join her on 25 January in Tahrir Square to bring down the Mubarak regime.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Mahfouz's use of <a href="/wiki/Video_blogging" class="mw-redirect" title="Video blogging">video blogging</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_media" title="Social media">social media</a> went <a href="/wiki/Viral_video" title="Viral video">viral</a><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> and urged people not to be afraid.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> The Facebook group for the event attracted 80,000 people.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id=".28Pre-.29revolution_timeline"><span id="Timeline"></span>(Pre-)revolution timeline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: (Pre-)revolution timeline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Farouk_to_Mubarak"><span id="From_King_Farouk_to_Mubarak"></span>Farouk to Mubarak</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Farouk to Mubarak">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most causes of the 2011 Egyptian revolution against Mubarak also existed in 1952, when the <a href="/wiki/Free_Officers_Movement_(Egypt)" title="Free Officers Movement (Egypt)">Free Officers</a> ousted <a href="/wiki/Farouk_of_Egypt" title="Farouk of Egypt">King Farouk</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> inherited power, corruption, under-development, unemployment, unfair distribution of wealth and the presence of Israel. A new cause of the Arab Spring is the increase in population, which increased unemployment. The first sign along the road to Mubarak was the 1967 <a href="/wiki/Six-Day_War" title="Six-Day War">war between Egypt and Israel</a>. <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a>'s defeat brought Anwar Sadat to power after Nasser's death in 1970. Sadat undid Nasser's social reforms and dependence on the Soviet Union, predicting its <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1989" title="Revolutions of 1989">collapse</a> nearly two decades before it occurred.</p>
<p>Sadat neglected the modernization of Egypt, and his cronyism cost the country infrastructure industries which could generate new jobs. He was succeeded by Hosni Mubarak after Sadat's 1981 death. With no academic or governmental experience, Mubarak implemented emergency rule throughout his 30 years in office, not appointing a vice president until he was pressured to resign. Communications media such as the internet, cell phones and satellite TV channels augmented mosques and Friday prayers, traditional means of mass communications. The mosques brought the Muslim Brotherhood to power, and the Brotherhood has pressured all governments from 1928 through 2011 (as it also does in neighboring countries).<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Under_Mubarak"><span id="Under_Hosni_Mubarak.27s_rule"></span>Under Mubarak</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Under Mubarak">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Egyptian_revolution_of_2011" title="Timeline of the Egyptian revolution of 2011">Timeline of the Egyptian revolution of 2011</a></div>
