</div>
<p><em>25 January 2011 ("Day of Revolt")</em>: Protests erupted throughout Egypt, with tens of thousands gathering in Cairo and thousands more in other Egyptian cities. The protests targeted the Mubarak government; while mostly non-violent, there were some reports of civilian and police casualties.</p>
<p><em>26 January 2011</em>: Civil unrest in Suez and other areas throughout the country. Police arrested many activists.</p>
<p><em>27 January 2011</em>: The government shuts down four major ISPs at approximately 5:20&#160;p.m.&#160;EST.<sup id="cite_ref-wired_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wired-117">[117]</a></sup> disrupting Internet traffic and telephone services<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>28 January 2011</em>: The "Friday of Anger" protests began, with hundreds of thousands demonstrating in Cairo and other Egyptian cities after Friday prayers. Opposition leader Mohamed ElBaradei arrived in Cairo amid reports of looting. Prisons were opened and burned down, allegedly on orders from Interior Minister Habib El Adly. Prison inmates escaped <i>en masse</i>, in what was believed to be an attempt to terrorise protesters. Police were withdrawn from the streets, and the military was deployed. International fears of violence grew, but no major casualties were reported. Mubarak made his first address to the nation, pledging to form a new government. Later that night clashes broke out in Tahrir Square between revolutionaries and pro-Mubarak demonstrators, leading to casualties.</p>
<p><em>29 January 2011</em>: The military presence in Cairo increased. A curfew was imposed, which was widely ignored as the flow of protesters into Tahrir Square continued through the night. The military reportedly refused to follow orders to fire live ammunition, exercising overall restraint; there were no reports of major casualties. On 31 January, Israeli media reported that the 9th, 2nd, and 7th Divisions of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Army" title="Egyptian Army">Egyptian Army</a> had been ordered into Cairo to help restore order.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>1 February 2011</em>: Mubarak made another televised address, offering several concessions. He pledged political reforms and said he would not run in the elections planned for September, but would remain in office to oversee a peaceful transition. Small-but-violent clashes began that night between pro- and anti-Mubarak groups.</p>
<p><em>2 February 2011 (Camel Incident)</em>: Violence escalated as waves of Mubarak supporters met anti-government protesters; some Mubarak supporters rode camels and horses into Tahrir Square, reportedly wielding sticks. Mubarak repeated his refusal to resign in interviews with several news agencies. Violence toward journalists and reporters escalated, amid speculation that it was encouraged by Mubarak to bring the protests to an end.</p>
<p><em>6 February 2011</em>: An interfaith service was held with <a href="/wiki/Copts" title="Copts">Egyptian Christians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Egypt" title="Islam in Egypt">Muslims</a> in Tahrir Square. Negotiations by Egyptian Vice President <a href="/wiki/Omar_Suleiman" title="Omar Suleiman">Omar Suleiman</a> and opposition representatives began during continuing protests throughout the country. The Egyptian army assumed greater security responsibilities, maintaining order and guarding The <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Museum" title="Egyptian Museum">Egyptian Museum of Antiquity</a>. Suleiman offered reforms, while others in Mubarak's regime accused foreign nations (including the U.S.) of interfering in Egypt's affairs.</p>
<p><em>10 February 2011</em>: Mubarak addressed the Egyptian people amid speculation of a military coup. Instead of resigning (which was widely expected), he said he would delegate some powers to Vice President Suleiman while remaining Egypt's head of state. Mubarak's statement was met with anger, frustration and disappointment, and in a number of cities there was an escalation in the number and intensity of demonstrations.</p>
<p><em>11 February 2011 ("Friday of Departure")</em>: Large protests continued in many cities, as Egyptians refused to accept Mubarak's concessions. At 6:00&#160;pm Suleiman announced Mubarak's resignation, entrusting the Supreme Council of Egyptian Armed Forces with the leadership of the country. Nationwide celebrations immediately followed.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-revolution_timeline">Post-revolution timeline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Post-revolution timeline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aftermath_of_the_2011_Egyptian_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Aftermath of the 2011 Egyptian revolution">Aftermath of the 2011 Egyptian revolution</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Under_the_Supreme_Council_of_the_Armed_Forces"><span id="Under_Supreme_Council_of_the_Armed_Forces"></span>Under the Supreme Council of the Armed Forces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Under the Supreme Council of the Armed Forces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_2011_Egyptian_revolution_under_Supreme_Council_of_the_Armed_Forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Timeline of the 2011 Egyptian revolution under Supreme Council of the Armed Forces">Timeline of the 2011 Egyptian revolution under Supreme Council of the Armed Forces</a></div>
<p><em>13 February 2011</em>: The <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Council_of_the_Armed_Forces" title="Supreme Council of the Armed Forces">Supreme Council</a> dissolved Egypt’s parliament and suspended the constitution in response to demands by demonstrators. The council declared that it would wield power for six months, or until elections could be held. Calls were made for the council to provide details and more-specific timetables and deadlines. Major protests subsided, but did not end. In a gesture to a new beginning, protesters cleaned up and renovated Tahrir Square (the epicenter of the demonstrations); however, many pledged to continue protesting until all demands had been met.</p>
<p><em>17 February</em>: The army said that it would not field a candidate in the upcoming presidential elections.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Four important figures in the former regime were arrested that day: former interior minister <a href="/wiki/Habib_el-Adly" title="Habib el-Adly">Habib el-Adly</a>, former minister of housing <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ahmed_Maghrabi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ahmed Maghrabi (page does not exist)">Ahmed Maghrabi</a>, former tourism minister H.E. <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zuheir_Garana&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Zuheir Garana (page does not exist)">Zuheir Garana</a> and steel tycoon <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Ezz_(businessman)" title="Ahmed Ezz (businessman)">Ahmed Ezz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>2 March</em>: The <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_constitutional_referendum,_2011" title="Egyptian constitutional referendum, 2011">constitutional referendum</a> was tentatively scheduled for 19 March 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>3 March</em>: A day before large protests against him were planned, <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Shafik" title="Ahmed Shafik">Ahmed Shafik</a> stepped down as prime minister and was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Essam_Sharaf" title="Essam Sharaf">Essam Sharaf</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>5 March</em>: Several <a href="/wiki/State_Security_Intelligence" class="mw-redirect" title="State Security Intelligence">State Security Intelligence</a> (SSI) buildings across Egypt were raided by protesters, including the headquarters for the <a href="/wiki/Alexandria_Governorate" title="Alexandria Governorate">Alexandria Governorate</a> and the national headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Nasr_City" title="Nasr City">Nasr City</a>, Cairo. Protesters said that they raided the buildings to secure documents they believed to proved crimes by the SSI against the people of Egypt during Mubarak's rule.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>6 March</em>: From the Nasr City headquarters, protesters acquired evidence of <a href="/wiki/Mass_surveillance" title="Mass surveillance">mass surveillance</a> and vote-rigging, noting rooms full of videotapes, piles of shredded and burned documents and cells in which activists recounted their experiences of detention and torture.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>19 March</em>: The constitutional referendum passed with 77.27 percent of the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>22 March</em>: Portions of the Interior Ministry building caught fire during police demonstrations outside.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>23 March</em>: The <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_Egypt" title="Cabinet of Egypt">Egyptian Cabinet</a> ordered a law criminalising protests and strikes which hamper work at private or public establishments. Under the new law, anyone organising such protests will be subject to imprisonment or a fine of <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_pound" title="Egyptian pound">EGP</a>500,000 (about USD$100,000).<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>1 April ("Save the Revolution Day")</em>: About 4,000 demonstrators filled Tahrir Square for the largest protest in weeks, demanding that the ruling military council more quickly dismantle lingering aspects of the old regime;<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> protestors also demanded trials for <a href="/wiki/Hosni_Mubarak" title="Hosni Mubarak">Hosni Mubarak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Mubarak" title="Gamal Mubarak">Gamal Mubarak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Fathi_Sorour" title="Ahmad Fathi Sorour">Ahmad Fathi Sorour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Safwat_El-Sherif" title="Safwat El-Sherif">Safwat El-Sherif</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zakaria_Azmi" title="Zakaria Azmi">Zakaria Azmi</a>.</p>
<p><em>8 April ("Cleansing Friday")</em>: Tens of thousands of demonstrators again filled Tahrir Square, criticizing the Supreme Council of the Armed Forces for not following through on their demands: the resignation of remaining regime figures and the removal of Egypt’s public prosecutor, due to the slow pace of investigations of corrupt former officials.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>7 May</em>: The <a href="/wiki/2011_Imbaba_church_attacks" title="2011 Imbaba church attacks">Imbaba church attacks</a>, in which <a href="/wiki/Salafi" class="mw-redirect" title="Salafi">Salafi</a> Muslims attacked <a href="/wiki/Copts" title="Copts">Coptic</a> Christian churches in the working-class neighborhood of <a href="/wiki/Imbaba" title="Imbaba">Imbaba</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>27 May ("Second Friday of Anger", "Second Revolution of Anger" or "The Second Revolution")</em>: Tens of thousands of demonstrators filled Tahrir Square,<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> in addition to demonstrations in Alexandria, Suez, Ismailia and Gharbeya, in the largest demonstrations since the ouster of the Mubarak regime. Protestors demanded no military trials for civilians, restoration of the Egyptian Constitution before parliament elections and for all members of the old regime (and those who killed protestors in January and February) to stand trial.</p>
<p><em>1 July ("Friday of Retribution")</em>: Thousands of protesters gathered in Suez, Alexandria and Tahrir Square to voice frustration with the ruling Supreme Council of the Armed Forces for what they called the slow pace of change, five months after the revolution, some also feared that the military is to rule Egypt indefinitely.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>8 July ("Friday of Determination")</em>: Hundreds of thousands of protesters gathered in Suez, Alexandria and Tahrir Square, demanding immediate reform and swifter prosecution of former officials from the ousted government.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>15 July</em>: Tahrir Square protests continued.</p>
<p><em>23 July</em>: Thousands of protesters attempted to march to the defense ministry after a speech by Mohammed Tantawi commemorating the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Revolution_of_1952" class="mw-redirect" title="Egyptian Revolution of 1952">Egyptian Revolution of 1952</a>, but are met with counter-insurgents with sticks, stones and Molotov cocktails.</p>
<p><em>1 August</em>: Egyptian soldiers clashed with protesters, tearing down tents. Sixty-six people were arrested, and most Egyptians supported the military's action.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><em>6 August</em>: Hundreds of protesters gathered and prayed in Tahrir Square before they were attacked by soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>9 September (<a href="/wiki/2011_Israeli_embassy_attack" class="mw-redirect" title="2011 Israeli embassy attack">2011 Israeli embassy attack</a>; the "Friday of Correcting the Path")</em>: Tens of thousands of people protested in Suez, Alexandria and Cairo; however, Islamist protesters were absent.</p>
<p><em>9 October (<a href="/wiki/Maspero_demonstrations" title="Maspero demonstrations">Maspero demonstrations</a>)</em>:<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Late in the evening of 9 October, during a protest in the <a href="/wiki/Maspiro_television_building" class="mw-redirect" title="Maspiro television building">Maspiro television building</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> peaceful Egyptian protesters calling for the dissolution the Supreme Council of the Armed Forces, the resignation of Chairman Field Marshal Mohamed Tantawi and the dismissal of the governor of Aswan province were attacked by military police. At least 25 people<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> were killed, and more than 200 wounded.</p>
<p><em>19 November</em>: Clashes erupted as demonstrators reoccupied Tahrir Square. Central Security Forces used <a href="/wiki/Tear_gas" title="Tear gas">tear gas</a> to control the situation.<sup id="cite_ref-facing_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facing-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>20 November</em>: Police attempted to forcibly clear the square, but protesters returned in more than double their original numbers. Fighting continued through the night, with police using tear gas, beating and shooting demonstrators.<sup id="cite_ref-facing_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facing-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>21 November</em>: Demonstrators returned to the square, with <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Christian" class="mw-redirect" title="Coptic Christian">Coptic Christians</a> standing guard as Muslims protesting the regime pause for prayers. The Health Ministry said that at least 23 died and over 1,500 were injured since 19 November.<sup id="cite_ref-facing_141-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facing-141">[141]</a></sup> Solidarity protests were held in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suez" title="Suez">Suez</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Dissident journalist <a href="/wiki/Hossam_el-Hamalawy" title="Hossam el-Hamalawy">Hossam el-Hamalawy</a> told <a href="/wiki/Al_Jazeera" title="Al Jazeera">Al Jazeera</a> that Egyptians would begin a <a href="/wiki/General_strike" title="General strike">general strike</a> because they "had enough" of the SCAF.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>28 November 2011 – 11 January 2012</em>: <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_parliamentary_election,_2011%E2%80%932012" class="mw-redirect" title="Egyptian parliamentary election, 2011–2012">Parliamentary elections</a></p>
<p><em>17 December 2011</em>: The Institute d'Egypte caught fire during clashes between protesters and Egyptian military; thousands of rare documents burned.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>23 January 2012</em>: Democratically-elected representatives of the People’s Assembly met for the first time since Egypt’s revolution, and the Supreme Council of the Armed Forces gave them legislative authority.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>24 January</em>: <a href="/wiki/Field_Marshal" class="mw-redirect" title="Field Marshal">Field Marshal</a> <a href="/wiki/Hussein_Tantawi" class="mw-redirect" title="Hussein Tantawi">Hussein Tantawi</a> said that the decades-old state of emergency would be partially lifted the following day.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>12 April</em>: An administrative court suspended the 100-member constitutional assembly tasked with drafting a new Egyptian constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>23–24 May</em>: First round of voting in the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_presidential_election,_2012" title="Egyptian presidential election, 2012">first presidential election</a> since Hosni Mubarak was deposed.</p>
<p><em>31 May</em>: The decades-long state of emergency expired.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>2 June</em>: Mubarak and his former interior minister <a href="/wiki/Habib_al-Adli" class="mw-redirect" title="Habib al-Adli">Habib al-Adli</a> were sentenced to life in prison because of their failure to stop the killing during the first six days of the revolution. The former president, his two sons and a business tycoon were acquitted of corruption charges because the <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_limitations" title="Statute of limitations">statute of limitations</a> had expired. Six senior police officials were also acquitted for their role in the killing of demonstrators, due to lack of evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>8 June</em>: Political factions tentatively agreed to a deal to form a new constitutional assembly, consisting of 100 members who will draft the new constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>12 June</em>: When the Egyptian parliament met to vote for members of a constitutional assembly dozens of secular MPs walked out, accusing Islamist parties of trying to dominate the panel.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>13 June</em>: After Egypt's military government imposed <i>de facto</i> martial law (extending the arrest powers of security forces), the Justice Ministry issued a decree giving military officers authority to arrest civilians and try them in military courts.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT20120618_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT20120618-165">[165]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> The provision remains in effect until a new constitution is introduced, and could mean those detained could remain in jail for that long according to state-run Egy News.<sup id="cite_ref-CNN_2012-06-14_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN_2012-06-14-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>14 June</em>: The Egyptian Supreme Constitutional Court ruled that a law passed by Parliament in May, banning former regime figures from running for office, was unconstitutional; this ended a threat to <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Shafik" title="Ahmed Shafik">Ahmed Shafik</a>'s candidacy for president during <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_presidential_election,_2012" title="Egyptian presidential election, 2012">Egypt's 2012 presidential election</a>. The court ruled that all articles making up the law regulating the 2011 parliamentary elections were invalid, upholding a lower-court ruling which found that candidates running on party slates were allowed to contest the one-third of parliamentary seats reserved for independents. The Egyptian parliament was dissolved, and the Supreme Council of the Armed Forces resumed legislative authority. The SCAF said that it would announce a 100-person assembly to write the country's new constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-CNN_2012-06-14_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN_2012-06-14-167">[167]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>15 June</em>: Security forces were stationed around Parliament to bar anyone, including lawmakers, from entering the chambers without official authorisation.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT20120617_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT20120617-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>16–17 June</em>: Second round of voting in the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_presidential_election,_2012" title="Egyptian presidential election, 2012">Egyptian presidential election</a>. The SCAF issued an interim constitution,<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WP20120618_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WP20120618-175">[175]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CNN20120618_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN20120618-176">[176]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Aljaz20120618_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aljaz20120618-177">[177]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ahram20120618_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahram20120618-178">[178]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> giving itself the power to control the prime minister, legislation, the national budget and declarations of war without oversight, and chose a 100-member panel to draft a permanent constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT20120617_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT20120617-173">[173]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ahram20120626_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahram20120626-182">[182]</a></sup> Presidential powers include the power to choose his vice president and cabinet, to propose the state budget and laws and to issue pardons.<sup id="cite_ref-Aljaz20120618_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aljaz20120618-177">[177]</a></sup> The interim constitution removed the military and the defense minister from presidential authority and oversight.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT20120618_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT20120618-165">[165]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Aljaz20120618_177-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aljaz20120618-177">[177]</a></sup> According to the interim constitution, a permanent constitution must be written within three months and be subject to a referendum 15 days later. When a permanent constitution is approved, a parliamentary election will be held within a month to replace the dissolved parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-WP20120618_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WP20120618-175">[175]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CNN20120618_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN20120618-176">[176]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Aljaz20120618_177-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aljaz20120618-177">[177]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ahram20120618_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahram20120618-178">[178]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>18 June</em>: The SCAF said that it picked a 100-member panel to draft a permanent constitution<sup id="cite_ref-NYT20120617_173-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT20120617-173">[173]</a></sup> if a court strikes down the parliament-picked assembly, planning a celebration at the end of June to mark the transfer of power to the new president.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT20120618_165-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT20120618-165">[165]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> Muslim Brotherhood candidate Mohamed Morsi declared himself the winner of the presidential election.<sup id="cite_ref-WP20120618_175-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WP20120618-175">[175]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CNN20120618_176-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN20120618-176">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>19–24 June</em>: Crowds gathered in <a href="/wiki/Tahrir_Square" title="Tahrir Square">Tahrir Square</a> to protest the SCAF's dissolution of an elected, Islamist parliament and await the outcome of the presidential election.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>24 June</em>: <a href="/wiki/Muslim_Brotherhood_in_Egypt" title="Muslim Brotherhood in Egypt">Muslim Brotherhood</a> candidate <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Morsi" title="Mohamed Morsi">Mohamed Morsi</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/Islamist" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamist">Islamist</a> elected head of an Arab state, is declared the winner of the presidential election by the Egyptian electoral commission.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>26 June</em>: The Supreme Administrative Court revoked Decree No. 4991/2012 from the Minister of Justice, which granted military intelligence and police the power to arrest civilians (a right previously reserved for civilian police officers).<sup id="cite_ref-Ahram20120626_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahram20120626-182">[182]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>27–28 June</em>: After the first <a href="/wiki/Constituent_Assembly_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Constituent Assembly of Egypt">Constituent Assembly of Egypt</a> was declared unconstitutional and dissolved in April by Egypt's Supreme Administrative Court, the second constituent assembly met to establish a framework for drafting a post-Mubarak constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>29 June</em>: Mohamed Morsi took a symbolic oath of office in Tahrir Square, affirming that the people are the source of power.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup></p>
<p><em>30 June</em>: Morsi was sworn in as Egypt's first democratically-elected president before the Supreme Constitutional Court at the podium used by U.S. President Barack Obama to reach out to the Islamic world in 2009 in his <a href="/wiki/A_New_Beginning" title="A New Beginning">A New Beginning</a> speech.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Under_President_Mohamed_Morsi">Under President Mohamed Morsi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Under President Mohamed Morsi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For a chronological summary of the major events which took place <i>after</i> the 2011–2012 Egyptian revolution under President Mohamed Morsi, see <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_2011%E2%80%932012_Egyptian_revolution_(Post-revolution_timeline)" class="mw-redirect" title="Timeline of the 2011–2012 Egyptian revolution (Post-revolution timeline)">Timeline of the 2011–2012 Egyptian revolution (Post-revolution timeline)</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="November_2012_declaration">November 2012 declaration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: November 2012 declaration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2012%E2%80%9313_Egyptian_protests" title="2012–13 Egyptian protests">2012–13 Egyptian protests</a></div>
<p>On 22 November 2012, Morsi issued a declaration immunizing his decrees from challenge and attempting to protect the work of the constituent assembly drafting the new constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> The declaration required a retrial of those acquitted of killing protesters, and extended the constituent assembly's mandate by two months. The declaration also authorized Morsi to take any measures necessary to protect the revolution. Liberal and secular groups walked out of the constituent assembly because they believed that it would impose strict Islamism, while the Muslim Brotherhood supported Morsi.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup></p>
<p>Morsi's declaration was criticized by Constitution Party leader <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_ElBaradei" title="Mohamed ElBaradei">Mohamed ElBaradei</a> (who said that he had "usurped all state powers and appointed himself Egypt's new <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaoh</a>"),<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> and led to violent protests throughout the country.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> Protesters again erected tents in <a href="/wiki/Tahrir_Square" title="Tahrir Square">Tahrir Square</a>, demanding a reversal of the declaration and the dissolving of the constituent assembly. A "huge protest" was planned for Tuesday, 27 November,<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> with clashes reported between protesters and police.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup> The declaration was also condemned by <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International UK</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2013 a youth group was created opposing Morsi and attempting to collect 22&#160;million signatures by 30 June 2013 (the first anniversary of his presidency) on a petition demanding early presidential elections. This triggered the June 2013 protests. Although protests were scheduled for 30 June, opponents began gathering on the 28th.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> Morsi supporters (primarily from Islamic parties) also protested that day.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> On 30 June the group organized large protests in Tahrir Square and the presidential palace demanding early presidential elections, which later spread to other <a href="/wiki/Governorates_of_Egypt" title="Governorates of Egypt">governorates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="June.E2.80.94July_2013_protests_and_overthrow">June—July 2013 protests and overthrow</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: June—July 2013 protests and overthrow">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/June_2013_Egyptian_protests" title="June 2013 Egyptian protests">June 2013 Egyptian protests</a> and <a href="/wiki/2013_Egyptian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="2013 Egyptian coup d'état">2013 Egyptian coup d'état</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div id="mwe_player_2" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:640px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_3&quot; poster=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Demonstration_in_Tahrir_Square_Against_Mubarak_-_30Jan2011.webm/640px--Demonstration_in_Tahrir_Square_Against_Mubarak_-_30Jan2011.webm.jpg&quot; <div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Demonstration_in_Tahrir_Square_Against_Mubarak_-_30Jan2011.webm" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Demonstration in Tahrir Square Against Mubarak - 30Jan2011</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 30 June 2013, marking the one-year anniversary of Mohamed Morsi's inauguration as president, millions of Egyptians protested against him, demanding he step down from office. Morsi refused to resign. A 48-hour ultimatum was issued to him, demanding that he respond to the demands of the Egyptians,<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> and on 3 July 2013, the President of Egypt was overthrown. Unlike the imposition of <a href="/wiki/Martial_law" title="Martial law">martial law</a> which followed the 2011 resignation of Hosni Mubarak, on 4 July 2013, a civilian senior jurist <a href="/wiki/Adly_Mansour" title="Adly Mansour">Adly Mansour</a> was appointed interim president and was sworn in over the new government following Morsi's removal. Mansour had the right to issue constitutional declarations and vested executive power in the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Constitutional_Court_of_Egypt" title="Supreme Constitutional Court of Egypt">Supreme Constitutional Court</a>, giving him executive, judicial and constitutional power.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> Morsi refused to accept his removal from office, and many supporters vowed to reinstate him. They originally intended their sit-ins to celebrate Morsi's one-year anniversary, but they quickly became opposed to the new authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> Their sit-ins <a href="/wiki/August_2013_Rabaa_massacre" title="August 2013 Rabaa massacre">were dispersed</a> on August 14 that year by security forces, leading to 638 deaths, including 43 police officers.<sup id="cite_ref-DeathToll-16-8-13_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeathToll-16-8-13-225">[225]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 18 January 2014, the interim government institutionalised a <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Constitution_of_2014" class="mw-redirect" title="Egyptian Constitution of 2014">new constitution</a> following a referendum in which 98.2% of voters were supportive. Participation was low with only 38.6% of registered voters participating<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> although this was higher than the 33% who voted in a referendum during Morsi's tenure.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> On 26 March 2014 <a href="/wiki/Abdel_Fattah_el-Sisi" title="Abdel Fattah el-Sisi">Abdel Fattah el-Sisi</a> the head of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Armed_Forces" title="Egyptian Armed Forces">Egyptian Armed Forces</a>, who at this time was in control of the country, resigned from the military, announcing he would stand as a candidate in the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_presidential_election,_2014" title="Egyptian presidential election, 2014">2014 presidential election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup> The poll, which had a 47% turnout, and was held between 26 and 28 May 2014, resulted in a resounding victory for el-Sisi.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> Sisi sworn into office as <a href="/wiki/President_of_Egypt" title="President of Egypt">President of Egypt</a> on 8 June 2014.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Protests_by_city"><span id="Protests_in_cities_and_regions"></span>Protests by city</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Protests by city">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Moe-_-_IMG_0262.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Seaside demonstration, with protesters wearing red, white and blue" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Flickr_-_Moe-_-_IMG_0262.jpg/220px-Flickr_-_Moe-_-_IMG_0262.jpg" width="220" height="81" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Flickr_-_Moe-_-_IMG_0262.jpg/330px-Flickr_-_Moe-_-_IMG_0262.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Flickr_-_Moe-_-_IMG_0262.jpg/440px-Flickr_-_Moe-_-_IMG_0262.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="379" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Moe-_-_IMG_0262.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Protesters in Alexandria</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cairo">Cairo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Cairo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Cairo has been at the epicentre of the revolution; the largest protests were held in downtown Tahrir Square, considered the "protest movement’s beating heart and most effective symbol."<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> During the first three days of the protests there were clashes between the central security police and demonstrators, but on 28 January the police withdrew from all of Cairo. Citizens formed neighbourhood-watch groups to maintain order, and widespread looting was reported. Traffic police were reintroduced to Cairo the morning of 31 January.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> An estimated two&#160;million people protested at Tahrir Square.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> During the protests, reporters Natasha Smith, <a href="/wiki/Lara_Logan" title="Lara Logan">Lara Logan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mona_Eltahawy" title="Mona Eltahawy">Mona Eltahawy</a> were <a href="/wiki/Rape_in_Egypt" title="Rape in Egypt">sexually assaulted</a> while covering the events.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alexandria">Alexandria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Alexandria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, home of <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Khaled_Mohamed_Saeed" title="Death of Khaled Mohamed Saeed">Khaled Saeed</a>, experienced major protests and clashes with police. There were few confrontations between demonstrators, since there were few Mubarak supporters (except for a few police-escorted convoys). The breakdown of law and order, including the general absence of police from the streets, continued until the evening of 3 February. Alexandria's protests were notable for the joint presence of Christians and Muslims in the events following the <a href="/wiki/2011_Alexandria_bombing" title="2011 Alexandria bombing">church bombing</a> on 1 January, which sparked protests against the Mubarak regime.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mansoura">Mansoura</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Mansoura">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the northern city of <a href="/wiki/Mansoura,_Egypt" title="Mansoura, Egypt">Mansoura</a>, there were daily protests against the Mubarak regime beginning on 25 January; two days later, the city was called a "war zone".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> On 28 January, 13 were reported dead in violent clashes; on 9 February, 18 more protesters died. One protest, on 1 February, had an estimated attendance of one million. The remote city of <a href="/wiki/Siwa_Oasis" title="Siwa Oasis">Siwa</a> had been relatively calm,<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> but local <a href="/wiki/Sheikhs" class="mw-redirect" title="Sheikhs">sheikhs</a> reportedly in control put the community under <a href="/wiki/Lockdown" title="Lockdown">lockdown</a> after a nearby town was <a href="/wiki/Arson" title="Arson">burned</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Suez">Suez</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Suez">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Suez" title="Suez">Suez</a> also saw violent protests. Eyewitness reports suggested that the death toll was high, although confirmation was difficult due to a ban on media coverage in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup> Some online activists called Suez Egypt's <a href="/wiki/Sidi_Bouzid" title="Sidi Bouzid">Sidi Bouzid</a> (the Tunisian city where protests began).<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup> On 3 February, 4,000 protesters took to the streets to demand Mubarak's resignation.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> A labour strike took place on 8 February,<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> and large protests were held on 11 February.<sup id="cite_ref-signonsandiego1_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-signonsandiego1-242">[242]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_cities">Other cities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Other cities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There were protests in <a href="/wiki/Luxor" title="Luxor">Luxor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> On 11 February police opened fire on protesters in <a href="/wiki/Dairut" title="Dairut">Dairut</a>, tens of thousands of protesters took to the streets of <a href="/wiki/Shebin_el-Kom" class="mw-redirect" title="Shebin el-Kom">Shebin el-Kom</a>, thousands protested in <a href="/wiki/El-Arish" class="mw-redirect" title="El-Arish">El-Arish</a> on the Sinai Peninsula,<sup id="cite_ref-signonsandiego1_242-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-signonsandiego1-242">[242]</a></sup> large protests took place in the southern cities of <a href="/wiki/Sohag" title="Sohag">Sohag</a> and <a href="/wiki/Minya,_Egypt" title="Minya, Egypt">Minya</a> and nearly 100,000 people protested in and around local-government headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Isma%C3%AFlia" class="mw-redirect" title="Ismaïlia">Ismaïlia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-signonsandiego1_242-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-signonsandiego1-242">[242]</a></sup> Over 100,000 protesters gathered on 27 January in front of the city council in <a href="/wiki/Zagazig" title="Zagazig">Zagazig</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bedouins" class="mw-redirect" title="Bedouins">Bedouins</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sinai_Peninsula" title="Sinai Peninsula">Sinai Peninsula</a> fought security forces for several weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup> As a result of the decreased military border presence, Bedouin groups protected the borders and pledged their support of the revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup> However, despite mounting tension among tourists no protests or civil unrest occurred in <a href="/wiki/Sharm-El-Sheikh" class="mw-redirect" title="Sharm-El-Sheikh">Sharm-El-Sheikh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Deaths">Deaths</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Deaths">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Martyrs_memorial_in_Tahrir.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Roped-off rectangle of framed photographs" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Martyrs_memorial_in_Tahrir.jpg/220px-Martyrs_memorial_in_Tahrir.jpg" width="220" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Martyrs_memorial_in_Tahrir.jpg/330px-Martyrs_memorial_in_Tahrir.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Martyrs_memorial_in_Tahrir.jpg/440px-Martyrs_memorial_in_Tahrir.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1924" data-file-height="1160" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Martyrs_memorial_in_Tahrir.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Tahrir Square memorial made by demonstrators in honour of those who died during the protests, regarded as <i><a href="/wiki/Shahid" title="Shahid">shuhada'</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">شهداء</span>‎‎ (martyrs). The photo captions attribute most of the deaths to police violence.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Graffiti_at_Tahrir_square.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Portraits of people on a wall, below deer-like animals" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Graffiti_at_Tahrir_square.jpg/220px-Graffiti_at_Tahrir_square.jpg" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Graffiti_at_Tahrir_square.jpg/330px-Graffiti_at_Tahrir_square.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Graffiti_at_Tahrir_square.jpg/440px-Graffiti_at_Tahrir_square.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1278" data-file-height="720" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Graffiti_at_Tahrir_square.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Graffiti at Tahrir square, commemorating martyrs of the revolution</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Before the protests six cases of <a href="/wiki/Self-immolation" title="Self-immolation">self-immolation</a> were reported, including a man arrested while trying to set himself afire in downtown Cairo.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> The cases were inspired by (and began one month after) the acts of self-immolation in Tunisia which triggered the Tunisian revolution. The self-immolators included Abdou Abdel-Moneim Jaafar,<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup> Mohammed Farouk Hassan,<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup> Mohammed Ashour Sorour<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup> and Ahmed Hashim al-Sayyed, who later died from his injuries.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 30 January, <i>Al Jazeera</i> reported as many as 150 deaths in the protests.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)" title="The Sun (United Kingdom)">The Sun</a></i> reported that the dead included at least ten policemen, three of whom were killed in <a href="/wiki/Rafah" title="Rafah">Rafah</a> by "an enraged mob".<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 29 January, 2,000 people were confirmed injured.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup> That day, an employee of the <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijani</a> embassy in Cairo was killed on their way home from work;<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup> the following day, Azerbaijan sent a plane to evacuate citizens<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup> and opened a criminal investigation into the killing.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup></p>
<p>Funerals for those killed during the "Friday of Anger" were held on 30 January. Hundreds of mourners gathered, calling for Mubarak's removal.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup> By 1 February the protests left at least 125 people dead,<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup> although Human Rights Watch said that UN High Commissioner for Human Rights <a href="/wiki/Navi_Pillay" title="Navi Pillay">Navi Pillay</a> claimed that as many as 300 might have died in the unrest. The unconfirmed tally included 80 Human-Rights-Watch-verified deaths at two Cairo hospitals, 36 in Alexandria and 13 in Suez;<sup id="cite_ref-latimes_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-latimes-261">[261]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-haaretz_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haaretz-263">[263]</a></sup> over 3,000 people were reported injured.<sup id="cite_ref-latimes_261-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-latimes-261">[261]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-haaretz_263-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haaretz-263">[263]</a></sup></p>
<p>An Egyptian governmental fact-finding commission about the revolution announced on 19 April that at least 846 Egyptians died in the nearly three-week-long uprising.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup> One prominent Egyptian who was killed was <a href="/wiki/Emad_Effat" title="Emad Effat">Emad Effat</a>, a senior cleric at the <a href="/wiki/Dar_al-Ifta_al-Misriyyah" title="Dar al-Ifta al-Misriyyah">Dar al-Ifta al-Misriyyah</a> school of <a href="/wiki/Al-Azhar_University" title="Al-Azhar University">Al-Azhar University</a>. He died 16 December 2011, after he was shot in front of the cabinet building.<sup id="cite_ref-senior_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-senior-267">[267]</a></sup> At Effat's funeral the following day, hundreds of mourners chanted "Down with military rule."<sup id="cite_ref-senior_267-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-senior-267">[267]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_reaction"><span id="International_reactions"></span>International reaction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: International reaction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/International_reactions_to_the_2011_Egyptian_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="International reactions to the 2011 Egyptian revolution">International reactions to the 2011 Egyptian revolution</a></div>
<p>International response to the protests was initially mixed,<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">[269]</a></sup> although most governments called for peaceful action on both sides and a move towards reform. Most Western nations expressed concern about the situation, and many governments issued travel advisories and attempted to evacuate their citizens from Egypt.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">[270]</a></sup></p>
<p>The European Union Foreign Affairs Chief said, "I also reiterate my call upon the Egyptian authorities to urgently establish a constructive and peaceful way to respond to the legitimate aspirations of Egyptian citizens for democratic and socioeconomic reforms."<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">[271]</a></sup> The United States, the United Kingdom, France and Germany issued similar statements calling for reform and an end to violence against peaceful protesters. Many states in the region expressed concern and supported Mubarak; Saudi Arabia issued a statement "strongly condemn[ing]" the protests,<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">[272]</a></sup> while Tunisia and Iran supported them. Israel was cautious, with <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Israel" title="Prime Minister of Israel">Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Netanyahu" title="Benjamin Netanyahu">Benjamin Netanyahu</a> asking his government ministers to maintain silence and urging Israel's allies to curb their criticism of President Mubarak;<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">[273]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">[274]</a></sup> however, an <a href="/wiki/Arab-Israeli" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab-Israeli">Arab-Israeli</a> parliamentarian supported the protests. Solidarity demonstrations for the protesters were held worldwide.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organization" title="Non-governmental organization">Non-governmental organizations</a> expressed concern about the protests and the heavy-handed state response, with <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a> describing attempts to discourage the protests as "unacceptable".<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup> Many countries (including the U.S., Israel, the UK and Japan) issued travel warnings or began evacuating their citizens, and <a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">multinational corporations</a> began evacuating <a href="/wiki/Expatriate" title="Expatriate">expatriate</a> employees.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">[276]</a></sup> Many university students were also evacuated.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-ouster"><span id="Post-ousting"></span>Post-ouster</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Post-ouster">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many nations, leaders and organizations hailed the end of the Mubarak regime, and celebrations were held in Tunisia and Lebanon. World leaders, including German Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Angela_Merkel" title="Angela Merkel">Angela Merkel</a> and UK Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a>, joined in praising the revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">[277]</a></sup> U.S. President Barack Obama praised the achievement of the Egyptian people and encouraged other activists, saying "Let's look at Egypt's example".<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">[278]</a></sup> Amid growing concern for the country, David Cameron was the first world leader to visit Egypt (10 days after Mubarak's resignation). A news blackout was lifted as the prime minister landed in Cairo for a brief five-hour stopover, hastily added to the beginning of a planned tour of the Middle East.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">[279]</a></sup> On 15 March, United States Secretary of State Hillary Clinton visited Egypt; she was the highest-ranking U.S. official to visit the country since the handover of power from Mubarak to the military. Clinton urged military leaders to begin the process of a democratic transition, offering support to protesters and reaffirming ties between the two nations.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">[280]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Results">Results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Domestic_responses_to_the_Egyptian_Revolution_of_2011" title="Domestic responses to the Egyptian Revolution of 2011">Domestic responses to the Egyptian Revolution of 2011</a></div>
<p>On 29 January Mubarak indicated that he would change the government because, despite the crossing of a "point of no return", national stability and <a href="/wiki/Law_and_order_(politics)" title="Law and order (politics)">law and order</a> must prevail. He asked the government, formed only months ago, to step down and promised that a new government would be formed.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">[281]</a></sup> Mubarak appointed <a href="/wiki/Omar_Suleiman" title="Omar Suleiman">Omar Suleiman</a>, head of Egyptian Intelligence, vice president and <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Shafik" title="Ahmed Shafik">Ahmed Shafik</a> prime minister.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">[282]</a></sup> On 1 February, he said he would stay in office until the next election in September, and then leave. Mubarak promised political reform, but made no offer to resign.</p>
<p>The Muslim Brotherhood joined the revolution on 30 January, calling on the military to intervene and all opposition groups to unite against Mubarak. It joined other opposition groups in electing Mohamed el Baradei to lead an interim government.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">[283]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many of the <a href="/wiki/Al-Azhar_Mosque" title="Al-Azhar Mosque">Al-Azhar</a> <a href="/wiki/Imam" title="Imam">imams</a> joined protesters throughout the country on 30 January.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup> Christian leaders asked their congregations not to participate in the demonstarations, although a number of young Christian activists joined protests led by <a href="/wiki/New_Wafd_Party" title="New Wafd Party">New Wafd Party</a> member <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Lakah" title="Raymond Lakah">Raymond Lakah</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">[285]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 31 January, Mubarak swore in his new cabinet in the hope that the unrest would fade. Protesters in <a href="/wiki/Tahrir_Square" title="Tahrir Square">Tahrir Square</a> continued demanding his ouster, since a vice-president and prime minister were already appointed.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">[286]</a></sup> He told the new government to preserve subsidies, control inflation and provide more jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">[287]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 1 February Mubarak said that although his candidacy had been announced by high-ranking members of his National Democratic Party,<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">[288]</a></sup> he never intended to run for reelection in September.<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">[289]</a></sup> He asked parliament for reforms:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>According to my constitutional powers, I call on parliament in both its houses to discuss amending article 76 and 77 of the constitution concerning the conditions on running for presidency of the republic and it sets specific a period for the presidential term. In order for the current parliament in both houses to be able to discuss these constitutional amendments and the legislative amendments linked to it for laws that complement the constitution and to ensure the participation of all the political forces in these discussions, I demand parliament to adhere to the word of the judiciary and its verdicts concerning the latest cases which have been legally challenged.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Hosni Mubarak, 1 February 2011<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">[290]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Opposition groups, including the Muslim Brotherhood (MB), repeated their demand that Mubarak resign; after the protests turned violent, the MB said that it was time for military intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">[291]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_ElBaradei" title="Mohamed ElBaradei">Mohamed ElBaradei</a>, who said he was ready to lead a transitional government,<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">[292]</a></sup> was a consensus candidate from a unified opposition, which included the <a href="/wiki/6_April_Youth_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="6 April Youth Movement">6 April Youth Movement</a>, the We Are All Khaled Said Movement, the <a href="/wiki/National_Association_for_Change" title="National Association for Change">National Association for Change</a>, the 25 January Movement, <a href="/wiki/Kefaya" title="Kefaya">Kefaya</a> and the Muslim Brotherhood.<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">[293]</a></sup> ElBaradei formed a "steering committee".<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">[294]</a></sup> On 5 February, talks began between the government and opposition groups for a transitional period before elections.</p>
<p>The government cracked down on the media, halting internet access<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">[295]</a></sup> (a primary means of opposition communication) with the help of London-based <a href="/wiki/Vodafone" title="Vodafone">Vodafone</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">[296]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">[297]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">[298]</a></sup> Journalists were harassed by supporters of the regime, eliciting condemnation from the <a href="/wiki/Committee_to_Protect_Journalists" title="Committee to Protect Journalists">Committee to Protect Journalists</a>, European countries and the United States. <a href="/wiki/Narus_(company)" title="Narus (company)">Narus</a>, a subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a>, sold the Mubarak government surveillance equipment to help identify dissidents.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">[299]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reforms"><span id="Reform_process"></span><span id="After-Revolution_Freedom_of_Establishing_Political_Parties"></span>Reforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Reforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_constitutional_review_committee_of_2011" title="Egyptian constitutional review committee of 2011">Egyptian constitutional review committee of 2011</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_political_parties_in_Egypt" title="List of political parties in Egypt">List of political parties in Egypt</a></div>
<p>The revolution's primary demands, chanted at every protest, were bread (jobs), freedom, social justice and human dignity. The fulfillment of these demands has been uneven and debatable. Demands stemming from the main four include the following:</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_sign_with_the_protesters_demands.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Large banner hanging from tall apartment building, with woman holding sign in foreground" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/A_sign_with_the_protesters_demands.jpg/170px-A_sign_with_the_protesters_demands.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/A_sign_with_the_protesters_demands.jpg/255px-A_sign_with_the_protesters_demands.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/A_sign_with_the_protesters_demands.jpg/340px-A_sign_with_the_protesters_demands.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_sign_with_the_protesters_demands.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sign with protester demands</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shreaded_paper_in_Egypt%27s_SS_HQ.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Cardboard box overflowing with shredded paper, with more shredded paper on floor" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Shreaded_paper_in_Egypt%27s_SS_HQ.jpg/170px-Shreaded_paper_in_Egypt%27s_SS_HQ.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Shreaded_paper_in_Egypt%27s_SS_HQ.jpg/255px-Shreaded_paper_in_Egypt%27s_SS_HQ.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Shreaded_paper_in_Egypt%27s_SS_HQ.jpg/340px-Shreaded_paper_in_Egypt%27s_SS_HQ.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shreaded_paper_in_Egypt%27s_SS_HQ.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Shredded documents at the State Security Investigations Service</div>
</div>
</div>
