<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Referendum_in_the_desert.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Referendum_in_the_desert.jpg/275px-Referendum_in_the_desert.jpg" width="275" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Referendum_in_the_desert.jpg/413px-Referendum_in_the_desert.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Referendum_in_the_desert.jpg/550px-Referendum_in_the_desert.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1080" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Referendum_in_the_desert.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Voter line in <a href="/wiki/Mokattam" title="Mokattam">Mokattam</a>, Cairo, during the 19 March 2011 <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_constitutional_referendum,_2011" title="Egyptian constitutional referendum, 2011">constitutional referendum</a> extending from the built-up area of Mokattam into the desert. The referendum had an unprecedented voter turnout (over 18&#160;million).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 17 February, an Egyptian prosecutor ordered the detention of three former ministers (interior minister Habib el-Adli, tourism minister Zuhair Garana and housing minister Ahmed el-Maghrabi) and steel magnate Ahmed Ezz pending trial for wasting public funds. The public prosecutor froze the bank accounts of Adli and his family following accusations that over 4&#160;million Egyptian pounds ($680,000) were transferred to his personal account by a businessman. The foreign minister was requested to contact European countries to freeze the other defendants' accounts.<sup id="cite_ref-AJEgypt_Ministers_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AJEgypt_Ministers-310">[310]</a></sup></p>
<p>That day, the United States announced that it would give Egypt $150&#160;million in aid to help it transition towards democracy. U.S. Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a> said that <a href="/wiki/William_Joseph_Burns" title="William Joseph Burns">William Burns</a> (undersecretary of state for political affairs) and <a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Lipton&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="David Lipton (page does not exist)">David Lipton</a> (a senior White House adviser on international economics) would travel to Egypt the following week.<sup id="cite_ref-AJEgypt_Ministers_310-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AJEgypt_Ministers-310">[310]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 19 February a moderate Islamic party which had been banned for 15 years, <a href="/wiki/Al-Wasat_Party" title="Al-Wasat Party">Al-Wasat Al-Jadid</a> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">حزب الوسط الجديد</span>‎‎, New Center Party), was finally recognised by an Egyptian court. The party was founded in 1996 by activists who split from the Muslim Brotherhood and sought to create a tolerant, liberal Islamic movement, but its four attempts to register as an official party were rejected. That day, Prime Minister Ahmed Shafiq also said that 222 political prisoners would be released. Shafiq said that only a few were detained during the uprising; he put the number of remaining political prisoners at 487, but did not say when they would be released.<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">[311]</a></sup> On 20 February <a href="/wiki/Yehia_El_Gamal" class="mw-redirect" title="Yehia El Gamal">Yehia El Gamal</a><sup class="noprint" style="font-style: normal;">[<a href="https://ar.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D9%8A%D8%AD%D9%8A%D9%89_%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%AC%D9%85%D9%84" class="extiw" title="ar:يحيى الجمل">ar</a>]</sup>, an activist and law professor, accepted on television the position of deputy prime minister. The next day, the Muslim Brotherhood announced that it would form a political party, the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_and_Justice_Party_(Egypt)" title="Freedom and Justice Party (Egypt)">Freedom and Justice Party</a> led by Saad Ketatni, for the upcoming parliamentary election.<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">[312]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">[313]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">[314]</a></sup> A spokesperson said, "When we talk about the slogans of the revolution – freedom, social justice, equality – all of these are in the <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a> (Islamic law)."<sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">[315]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 3 March, Prime Minister Shafiq submitted his resignation to the Supreme Council of the Armed Forces. The SCAF appointed <a href="/wiki/Essam_Sharaf" title="Essam Sharaf">Essam Sharaf</a>, a former transportation minister and a vocal critic of the regime following his resignation after the 2006 <a href="/wiki/Qalyoub_rail_accident" class="mw-redirect" title="Qalyoub rail accident">Qalyoub rail accident</a>, to replace Shafik and form a new government. Sharaf's appointment was seen as a concession to protesters, since he was actively involved in the events in Tahrir Square.<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">[316]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">[317]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">[318]</a></sup> Sharaf appointed former <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a> judge <a href="/wiki/Nabil_Elaraby" title="Nabil Elaraby">Nabil Elaraby</a> foreign minister and Mansour El Essawi as interior minister.<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">[319]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">[320]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 16 April the Higher Administrative Court dissolved the former ruling National Democratic Party (NDP), ordering its funds and property to be transferred to the government.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">[321]</a></sup> On 24 May it was announced that Hosni Mubarak and his sons, Gamal and Alaa, would be for over the deaths of anti-government protesters during the revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">[322]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trials"><span id="Court_trials_of_state_officials_accused_of_corruption"></span>Trials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Trials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trials_and_judicial_hearings_following_the_2011_Egyptian_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Trials and judicial hearings following the 2011 Egyptian revolution">Trials and judicial hearings following the 2011 Egyptian revolution</a></div>
<p>Mubarak's resignation was followed by a series of arrests of, and travel bans on, high-profile figures on charges of causing the deaths of 300–500 demonstrators, injuring 5,000 more, <a href="/wiki/Embezzlement" title="Embezzlement">embezzlement</a>, <a href="/wiki/Profiteering_(business)" title="Profiteering (business)">profiteering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Money_laundering" title="Money laundering">money laundering</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_abuses" class="mw-redirect" title="Human rights abuses">human rights abuses</a>. Among those charged were Mubarak, his wife <a href="/wiki/Suzanne_Mubarak" title="Suzanne Mubarak">Suzanne</a>, his sons Gamal and Alaa, former interior minister <a href="/wiki/Habib_el-Adly" title="Habib el-Adly">Habib el-Adly</a>, former housing minister Ahmed El-Maghrabi, former tourism minister <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zoheir_Garana&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Zoheir Garana (page does not exist)">Zoheir Garana</a> and former secretary for organizational affairs of the National Democratic Party Ahmed Ezz.<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">[323]</a></sup> Mubarak's ouster was followed by allegations of corruption against other government officials and senior politicians.<sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">[324]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">[325]</a></sup> On 28 February 2011, Egypt's top prosecutor ordered an <a href="/wiki/Asset_freezing" title="Asset freezing">assets freeze</a> on Mubarak and his family.<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">[326]</a></sup> This was followed by arrest warrants, travel bans and asset freezes for other public figures, including former parliament speaker <a href="/wiki/Fathi_Sorour" class="mw-redirect" title="Fathi Sorour">Fathi Sorour</a> and former <a href="/wiki/Shura_Council" title="Shura Council">Shura Council</a> speaker <a href="/wiki/Safwat_El_Sherif" class="mw-redirect" title="Safwat El Sherif">Safwat El Sherif</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">[327]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">[328]</a></sup> Arrest warrants were issued for financial misappropriations by public figures who left the country at the outbreak of the revolution, including former trade and industry minister <a href="/wiki/Rachid_Mohamed_Rachid" title="Rachid Mohamed Rachid">Rachid Mohamed Rachid</a> and businessman <a href="/wiki/Hussein_Salem" title="Hussein Salem">Hussein Salem</a>; Salem was believed to have fled to Dubai.<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">[329]</a></sup> Trials of the accused officials began on 5 March 2011, when former interior minister Habib el-Adli appeared at the Giza Criminal Court in Cairo.<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">[330]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 2011 <a href="/wiki/Abbud_al-Zumar" title="Abbud al-Zumar">Abbud al-Zumar</a>, one of Egypt's best-known political prisoners, was freed after 30 years. Founder and first <a href="/wiki/Emir" title="Emir">emir</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Islamic_Jihad" title="Egyptian Islamic Jihad">Egyptian Islamic Jihad</a>, he was implicated in the 6 October 1981 <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Anwar_Sadat" title="Assassination of Anwar Sadat">assassination of Anwar Sadat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">[331]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 24 May, Mubarak was ordered to stand trial on charges of <a href="/wiki/Premeditated_murder" class="mw-redirect" title="Premeditated murder">premeditated murder</a> of peaceful protestors during the revolution; if convicted, he could face the death penalty. The list of charges, released by the public prosecutor, was "intentional murder, attempted killing of some demonstrators&#160;... misuse of influence and deliberately wasting public funds and unlawfully making private financial gains and profits."<sup id="cite_ref-reutersmurdercharges_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutersmurdercharges-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis">Analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_instability">Regional instability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Regional instability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a></div>
<p>The Egyptian and Tunisian revolutions sparked a <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_wave" title="Revolutionary wave">wave of uprisings</a>, with demonstrations spreading across the <a href="/wiki/MENA" title="MENA">Middle East and North Africa</a>. <a href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%932011_Algerian_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2010–2011 Algerian protests">Algeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/2011_Bahraini_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2011 Bahraini protests">Bahrain</a>, <a href="/wiki/2011_Iranian_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2011 Iranian protests">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/2011_Jordanian_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2011 Jordanian protests">Jordan</a>, <a href="/wiki/2011_Libyan_civil_war" class="mw-redirect" title="2011 Libyan civil war">Libya</a>, <a href="/wiki/2011_Morocco_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2011 Morocco protests">Morocco</a>, <a href="/wiki/2011_Yemeni_uprising" class="mw-redirect" title="2011 Yemeni uprising">Yemen</a> and <a href="/wiki/2011_Syrian_uprising" class="mw-redirect" title="2011 Syrian uprising">Syria</a> witnessed major protests, and minor demonstrations occurred in <a href="/wiki/2011_Iraqi_protests" title="2011 Iraqi protests">Iraq</a>, Kuwait, <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a>, <a href="/wiki/2011_Omani_protests" title="2011 Omani protests">Oman</a>, <a href="/wiki/2011_Saudi_Arabian_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2011 Saudi Arabian protests">Saudi Arabia</a>, Somalia<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and <a href="/wiki/2011_Sudanese_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2011 Sudanese protests">Sudan</a>.</p>
<p>The Egyptian protests in Egypt were not centred around religion-based politics, but nationalism and social consciousness.<sup id="cite_ref-332" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-332">[332]</a></sup> Before the uprising, the best-organised and most-prominent opposition movements in the Arab world usually came from Islamist organisations with members who were motivated and ready to sacrifice. However, secular forces emerged from the revolution espousing principles shared with religious groups: freedom, social justice and dignity. Islamist organisations emerged with a greater freedom to operate. Although the cooperative, inter-faith revolution was no guarantee that partisan politics would not re-emerge in its wake, its success represented a change from the intellectual stagnation (created by decades of repression) which pitted modernity and Islamism against one another. Islamists and secularists are faced with new opportunities for dialogue on subjects such as the role of Islam and Sharia in society, freedom of speech and the impact of secularism on a predominantly-Muslim population.<sup id="cite_ref-333" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-333">[333]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite the optimism surrounding the revolution, commentators expressed concern about the risk of increased power and influence for Islamist forces in the country and region and the difficulty of integrating different groups, ideologies and visions for the country. Journalist <a href="/wiki/Caroline_Glick" title="Caroline Glick">Caroline Glick</a> wrote that the Egyptian revolution foreshadowed a rise in religious radicalism and support for terrorism, citing a 2010 <a href="/wiki/Pew" title="Pew">Pew</a> Opinion poll which found that Egyptians supported Islamists over modernizers by an over two-to-one margin.<sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334">[334]</a></sup> Another journalist, <a href="/wiki/Shlomo_Ben-Ami" title="Shlomo Ben-Ami">Shlomo Ben-Ami</a>, said that Egypt's most formidable task was to refute the old paradigm of the Arab world which sees the only choices for regimes repressive, secular dictatorships or repressive theocracies. Ben-Ami noted that with Islam a central part of the society, any emergent regime was bound to be attuned to religion. In his view, a democracy which excluded all religion from public life (as in France) could succeed in Egypt but no genuine Arab democracy could disallow the participation of political Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-Shlomo_Ben_Ari_335-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shlomo_Ben_Ari-335">[335]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since the revolution Islamist parties (such as the Muslim Brotherhood) have strengthened in the democratic landscape, leading constitutional change, voter mobilization and protests.<sup id="cite_ref-336" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-336">[336]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-337" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-337">[337]</a></sup> This was a concern of the secular and youth movements, who wanted elections to be held later so they could catch up to the already-well-organized groups. Elections were held in September 2011, with Liberty and Justice (the Muslim Brotherhood party) winning 48.5 percent of the vote. In 2014 in Upper Egypt, several newspapers reported that <a href="/wiki/Upper_Egypt" title="Upper Egypt">Upper Egypt</a> wanted to secede from the rest of the country to improve its standard of living.<sup id="cite_ref-338" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-338">[338]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alexandria_church_bombing">Alexandria church bombing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Alexandria church bombing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2011_Alexandria_bombing" title="2011 Alexandria bombing">2011 Alexandria bombing</a></div>
<p>Early on New Year's Day 2011 a bomb exploded in front of an Alexandria church, killing 23 <a href="/wiki/Copts" title="Copts">Coptic</a> Christians. Egyptian officials said that "foreign elements" were behind the attack.<sup id="cite_ref-339" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-339">[339]</a></sup> Some Copts accused the Egyptian government of negligence;<sup id="cite_ref-340" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-340">[340]</a></sup> after the attack, many Christians protested in the streets (with Muslims joining later). After clashing with police, protesters in Alexandria and Cairo shouted slogans denouncing Mubarak's rule<sup id="cite_ref-341" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-341">[341]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-342" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-342">[342]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-343" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-343">[343]</a></sup> in support of unity between Christians and Muslims. Their sense of being let down by national security forces was cited as one of the first grievances sparking the 25 January uprising.<sup id="cite_ref-344" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-344">[344]</a></sup> On 7 February a complaint was filed against Habib al-Adly (interior minister until Mubarak dissolved the government during the protests' early days, accusing him of directing the attack.<sup id="cite_ref-345" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-345">[345]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_women"><span id="Women.27s_role"></span>Role of women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Role of women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Floris_Van_Cauwelaert_-_The_messages_on_Tahrir_Square_(8).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Woman covered in black except for her eyes, holding a sign" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Flickr_-_Floris_Van_Cauwelaert_-_The_messages_on_Tahrir_Square_%288%29.jpg/220px-Flickr_-_Floris_Van_Cauwelaert_-_The_messages_on_Tahrir_Square_%288%29.jpg" width="220" height="331" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Flickr_-_Floris_Van_Cauwelaert_-_The_messages_on_Tahrir_Square_%288%29.jpg/330px-Flickr_-_Floris_Van_Cauwelaert_-_The_messages_on_Tahrir_Square_%288%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Flickr_-_Floris_Van_Cauwelaert_-_The_messages_on_Tahrir_Square_%288%29.jpg/440px-Flickr_-_Floris_Van_Cauwelaert_-_The_messages_on_Tahrir_Square_%288%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1424" data-file-height="2144" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Floris_Van_Cauwelaert_-_The_messages_on_Tahrir_Square_(8).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Female protester wearing a <a href="/wiki/Niq%C4%81b_in_Egypt" title="Niqāb in Egypt">niqāb</a><sup id="cite_ref-346" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-346">[346]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Women_in_Egypt" title="Women in Egypt">Egyptian women</a> have been participating actively in the revolution, in the same way that they played an active role in the strike movement in the few last years, in several cases pressurizing the men to join the strikes.<sup id="cite_ref-marxist.com_347-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-marxist.com-347">[347]</a></sup> In earlier protests in Egypt, women only accounted for about 10 per cent of the protesters, but on Tahrir Square they accounted for about 40 to 50 per cent in the days leading up to the fall of Mubarak. Women, with and without veils, participated in the defence of the square, set up barricades, led debates, shouted slogans and, together with the men, risked their lives.<sup id="cite_ref-marxist.com_347-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-marxist.com-347">[347]</a></sup> Some participated in the protests, were present in news clips and on Facebook forums and were part of the revolution's leadership during the Egyptian revolution. In Tahrir Square, female protesters (some with children) supported the protests. The diversity of the protesters in Tahrir Square was visible in the women who participated; many wore <a href="/wiki/Head_scarves" class="mw-redirect" title="Head scarves">head scarves</a> and other signs of religious conservatism, while others felt free to kiss a friend or smoke a cigarette in public. Women organised protests and reported events; female bloggers, such as Leil Zahra Mortada, risked abuse or imprisonment by keeping the world informed of events in Tahrir Square and elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Middle_East.E2.80.99s_Feminist_Revolution_348-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Middle_East.E2.80.99s_Feminist_Revolution-348">[348]</a></sup> Among those who died was Sally Zahran, who was beaten to death during one of the demonstrations. <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> reportedly planned to name one of its <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> exploration spacecraft in Zahran's honour.<sup id="cite_ref-349" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-349">[349]</a></sup></p>
<p>The participation and contributions by Egyptian women to the protests were attributed to the fact that many (especially younger women) were better educated than previous generations and represent more than half of Egyptian university students. This is an empowering factor for women, who have become more present and active publicly. The advent of social media also provided a tool for women to become protest leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Middle_East.E2.80.99s_Feminist_Revolution_348-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Middle_East.E2.80.99s_Feminist_Revolution-348">[348]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_the_military"><span id="The_military.27s_role"></span>Role of the military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Role of the military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burning_amy_vehicle.png" class="image"><img alt="A truck ablaze" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Burning_amy_vehicle.png/220px-Burning_amy_vehicle.png" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Burning_amy_vehicle.png/330px-Burning_amy_vehicle.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Burning_amy_vehicle.png/440px-Burning_amy_vehicle.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="598" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burning_amy_vehicle.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
One of two army vehicles burnt during the army attacks on 9 April 2011</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Armed_Forces" title="Egyptian Armed Forces">Egyptian Armed Forces</a> initially enjoyed a better public reputation than the police did; the former was seen as a professional body protecting the country, and the latter was accused of <a href="/wiki/Systemic_corruption" class="mw-redirect" title="Systemic corruption">systemic corruption</a> and lawless violence. However, when the SCAF cracked down on protesters after becoming the <i>de facto</i> ruler of Egypt the military's popularity decreased.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> All four <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_president" class="mw-redirect" title="Egyptian president">Egyptian presidents</a> since the 1950s have a military background. Key Egyptian military personnel include defense minister <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Hussein_Tantawi" title="Mohamed Hussein Tantawi">Mohamed Hussein Tantawi</a> and armed forces chief of staff <a href="/wiki/Sami_Hafez_Enan" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami Hafez Enan">Sami Hafez Enan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-350" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-350">[350]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-351" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-351">[351]</a></sup> The Egyptian military numbers about 468,500 active personnel, plus a reserve of 479,000.<sup id="cite_ref-352" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-352">[352]</a></sup></p>
<p>As head of Egypt's armed forces, Tantawi has been described as "aged and change-resistant" and is attached to the old regime. He has used his position as defense minister to oppose economic and political reform he saw as weakening central authority. Other key figures (<a href="/wiki/Sami_Hafez_Anan" title="Sami Hafez Anan">Sami Hafez Anan</a> chief among them) are younger, with closer connections to the U.S. and the Muslim Brotherhood. An important aspect of the relationship between the Egyptian and American military establishments is the $1.3&#160;billion in annual military aid provided to Egypt, which pays for American-made military equipment and allows Egyptian officers to train in the U.S. Guaranteed this aid package, the ruling SCAF is resistant to reform.<sup id="cite_ref-353" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-353">[353]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-354" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-354">[354]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-355" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-355">[355]</a></sup> One analyst, conceding the military's conservatism, says it has no option but to facilitate democratisation. It will have to limit its political role to continue good relations with the West, and cannot restrict Islamist participation in a genuine democracy.<sup id="cite_ref-Shlomo_Ben_Ari_335-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shlomo_Ben_Ari-335">[335]</a></sup></p>
<p>The military has led a violent crackdown on the Egyptian revolution since the fall of Mubarak. On 9 March 2011 military police violently dispersed a sit-in in Tahrir Square, arresting and torturing protesters. Seven female protesters were forcibly subjected to <a href="/wiki/Virginity_test" title="Virginity test">virginity tests</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-356" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-356">[356]</a></sup> During the night of 8 April 2011 military police attacked a sit-in in Tahrir Square by protesters and sympathetic military officers, killing at least one.<sup id="cite_ref-357" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-357">[357]</a></sup> On 9 October the Egyptian military crushed protesters under armed personnel carriers and shot live ammunition at a demonstration in front of the <a href="/wiki/Maspero_television_building" title="Maspero television building">Maspero television building</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maspero_demonstrations" title="Maspero demonstrations">killing at least 24</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-358" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-358">[358]</a></sup> On 19 November the military and police engaged in a continuous six-day battle with protestors in the streets of downtown Cairo and <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, killing nearly 40 and injuring over 2,000.<sup id="cite_ref-359" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-359">[359]</a></sup> On 16 December 2011 military forces dispersed a <a href="/wiki/Sit-in" title="Sit-in">sit-in</a> at the Cabinet of Ministers building, killing 17.<sup id="cite_ref-360" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-360">[360]</a></sup> Soldiers fired live ammunition and attacked from the rooftop with Molotov cocktails, rocks and other missiles.<sup id="cite_ref-361" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-361">[361]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_on_foreign_relations"><span id="Foreign_relations"></span>Impact on foreign relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Impact on foreign relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Egypt" title="Foreign relations of Egypt">Foreign relations of Egypt</a></div>
<p>Foreign governments in the West (including the U.S.) regarded Mubarak as an important ally and <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Egypt#Israeli-Palestinian_conflict" title="Foreign relations of Egypt">supporter</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Israeli-Palestinian_conflict" class="mw-redirect" title="Israeli-Palestinian conflict">Israeli-Palestinian</a> peace negotiations.<sup id="cite_ref-Hosni_Mubarak_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hosni_Mubarak-50">[50]</a></sup> After wars with <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> in 1948, 1956, 1967 and 1973, Egypt signed a <a href="/wiki/Egypt%E2%80%93Israel_Peace_Treaty" title="Egypt–Israel Peace Treaty">peace treaty</a> in 1979 (provoking controversy in the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a>). According to the 1978 <a href="/wiki/Camp_David_Accords" title="Camp David Accords">Camp David Accords</a> (which led to the peace treaty), Israel and Egypt receive billions of dollars in aid annually from the United States; Egypt received over US$1.3&#160;billion in military aid each year, in addition to economic and development assistance.<sup id="cite_ref-362" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-362">[362]</a></sup> According to Juan Cole many Egyptian youth felt ignored by Mubarak, feeling that he put the interests of the West ahead of theirs.<sup id="cite_ref-363" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-363">[363]</a></sup> The cooperation of the Egyptian regime in enforcing the <a href="/wiki/Blockade_of_the_Gaza_Strip" title="Blockade of the Gaza Strip">blockade of the Gaza Strip</a> was deeply unpopular with the Egyptian public.<sup id="cite_ref-364" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-364">[364]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Online_activism_and_social_media"><span id="Online_activism_and_the_role_of_social_media"></span>Online activism and social media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Online activism and social media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2011_Egyptian_protests_Facebook_%26_jan25_card.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Red, white and blue placard in English and Arabic, with a Twitter hashtag" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/2011_Egyptian_protests_Facebook_%26_jan25_card.jpg/220px-2011_Egyptian_protests_Facebook_%26_jan25_card.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/2011_Egyptian_protests_Facebook_%26_jan25_card.jpg/330px-2011_Egyptian_protests_Facebook_%26_jan25_card.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/2011_Egyptian_protests_Facebook_%26_jan25_card.jpg/440px-2011_Egyptian_protests_Facebook_%26_jan25_card.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2353" data-file-height="1669" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2011_Egyptian_protests_Facebook_%26_jan25_card.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Man holding a poster reading "Facebook, #jan25, The Egyptian Social Network" during the 2011 protests</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:April_2008_uprisings_in_Mahalla,_Egypt.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/April_2008_uprisings_in_Mahalla%2C_Egypt.JPG/220px-April_2008_uprisings_in_Mahalla%2C_Egypt.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/April_2008_uprisings_in_Mahalla%2C_Egypt.JPG/330px-April_2008_uprisings_in_Mahalla%2C_Egypt.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/April_2008_uprisings_in_Mahalla%2C_Egypt.JPG/440px-April_2008_uprisings_in_Mahalla%2C_Egypt.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:April_2008_uprisings_in_Mahalla,_Egypt.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
People take to the streets on 7 April 2008, in Mahalla, Egypt. In the days following the planned strike on 6 April that was shut down by government force, a series of uprisings and military reprisals turned the city of Mahalla, about two hours north of Cairo, into a conflict zone. Rising food prices fueled the unrest. The 6 April Movement was formed in the wake of the uprisings which fed revolutionary sentiment and helped lead to the 2011 revolution.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/6_April_Youth_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="6 April Youth Movement">6 April Youth Movement</a> (Arabic: حركة شباب 6 أبريل) is an Egyptian Facebook group begun in spring 2008 to support workers in El-Mahalla El-Kubra, an industrial town, who were planning to <a href="/wiki/2008_Egyptian_general_strike" title="2008 Egyptian general strike">strike on 6 April</a>. Activists called on participants to wear black and stay home the day of the strike. Bloggers and citizen journalists used Facebook, Twitter, Flickr, blogs and other media tools to report on the strike, alert their networks about police activity, organize legal protection and draw attention to their efforts. The New York Times has called it the political Facebook group in Egypt with the most dynamic debates.<sup id="cite_ref-365" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-365">[365]</a></sup> In March 2012 it had 325,000<sup id="cite_ref-366" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-366">[366]</a></sup> predominantly young and members, most previously inactive politically, whose concerns included free speech, nepotism in government and the country's stagnant economy. Their Facebook forum features intense and heated discussions, and is frequently updated.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/We_are_all_Khaled_Said" class="mw-redirect" title="We are all Khaled Said">We are all Khaled Said</a> is a Facebook group which formed in the aftermath of Said's beating and death. The group attracted hundreds of thousands of members worldwide, playing a prominent role in spreading (and drawing attention to) the growing discontent. As the protests began, Google executive <a href="/wiki/Wael_Ghonim" title="Wael Ghonim">Wael Ghonim</a> revealed that he was behind the account.<sup id="cite_ref-367" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-367">[367]</a></sup> In a TV interview with SCAF members after the revolution, Abdul Rahman Mansour (an underground activist and media expert) was disclosed as sharing the account with Ghonim.<sup id="cite_ref-368" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-368">[368]</a></sup> Another online contribution was made by <a href="/wiki/Asmaa_Mahfouz" title="Asmaa Mahfouz">Asmaa Mahfouz</a>, an activist who posted a video challenging people to publicly protest.<sup id="cite_ref-369" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-369">[369]</a></sup> Facebook had previously suspended the group because some administrators were using pseudonyms, a violation of the company's terms of service.<sup id="cite_ref-370" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-370">[370]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Social_media" title="Social media">Social media</a> has been used extensively.<sup id="cite_ref-371" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-371">[371]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-372" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-372">[372]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-373" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-373">[373]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-374" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-374">[374]</a></sup> As one Egyptian activist tweeted during the protests, "We use Facebook to schedule the protests, Twitter to coordinate, and YouTube to tell the world."<sup id="cite_ref-375" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-375">[375]</a></sup> Internet censorship has also been extensive, in some cases to the extent of taking entire nations virtually offline.<sup id="cite_ref-376" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-376">[376]</a></sup></p>
<p>Facebook, Twitter and blogging helped spread the uprising. Egyptian businessman Khaled Said was beaten to death by police in June 2010, reportedly in retaliation for a video he posted showing Egyptian police sharing the spoils of a drug bust. Wael Ghonim's memorial Facebook page to Said grew to over 400,000 followers, creating an online arena where protestors and those discontented with the government could gather and organise. The page called for protests on 25 January, later known as the "Day of Wrath". Hundreds of thousands of protestors flooded the streets to show their discontent with murder and corruption in their country. Ghonim was jailed on 28 January, and released 12 days later.</p>
<p>Egyptian activist and 6 April Youth Movement member Asmaa Mahfouz posted a video urging the Egyptian people to meet her at Tahrir Square, rise up against the government and demand democracy. In the video, she spoke about four protesters who had immolated themselves in protest of 30 years of poverty and degradation. On 24 January Mahfouz posted another video relating efforts made in support of the protest, from printing posters to creating flyers. The videos were posted on Facebook and then YouTube. The day after her last video post, hundreds of thousands of Egyptians poured into the streets in protest.</p>
<p>Since 25 January 2011, videos (including those of a badly-beaten Khaled Said, disproving police claims that he had choked to death), tweets and Facebook comments have kept the world abreast of the situation in Egypt. Amir Ali documents the ways in which social media was used by Egyptian activists, Egyptian celebrities and political figures abroad to fan the protests.<sup id="cite_ref-377" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-377">[377]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Democracy_Now!" title="Democracy Now!">Democracy Now!</a> journalist <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharif_Abdel_Kouddous&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sharif Abdel Kouddous (page does not exist)">Sharif Abdel Kouddous</a> provided live coverage and <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">tweets</a> from <a href="/wiki/Tahrir_Square" title="Tahrir Square">Tahrir Square</a> during the protests, and was credited with using social media to increase awareness of the protests.<sup id="cite_ref-378" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-378">[378]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-379" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-379">[379]</a></sup> The role of social media in the Egyptian uprising was debated in the first edition of the <a href="/wiki/Dubai_Debates" title="Dubai Debates">Dubai Debates</a>: "Mark Zuckerberg – the new hero of the Arab people?" <sup id="cite_ref-380" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-380">[380]</a></sup> Amir Ali has argued that, based in part on the Egyptian revolution, social media may be an effective tool in developing nations.<sup id="cite_ref-381" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-381">[381]</a></sup></p>
<p>Critics who downplay the influence of social networking on the Arab Spring cite several points:<sup id="cite_ref-382" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-382">[382]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Fewer than 20 percent of Egyptians had internet access, and the internet reached less than 40 percent of the country<sup id="cite_ref-383" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-383">[383]</a></sup></li>
<li>Social-networking sites were generally unpopular in the Middle East,<sup id="cite_ref-384" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-384">[384]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-385" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-385">[385]</a></sup></li>
<li>Such sites were not sufficiently private to evade authorities<sup id="cite_ref-386" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-386">[386]</a></sup></li>
<li>Many people did not trust social networking as a news source<sup id="cite_ref-387" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-387">[387]</a></sup></li>
<li>Social-networking sites were promoted by the media<sup id="cite_ref-388" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-388">[388]</a></sup></li>
<li>Social-networking sites did not involve non-activists in the revolution<sup id="cite_ref-389" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-389">[389]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Some protesters discouraged the use of social media. A widely circulated pamphlet by an anonymous activist group titled "How to Protest Intelligently" (Arabic: كيف للاحتجاج بذكاء؟), asked readers "not to use Twitter or Facebook or other websites because they are all being monitored by the Ministry of the Interior."<sup id="cite_ref-390" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-390">[390]</a></sup></p>
<p>Television, particularly live coverage by <i><a href="/wiki/Al_Jazeera_English" title="Al Jazeera English">Al Jazeera English</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/BBC_News" title="BBC News">BBC News</a>, was important to the revolution; the cameras provided exposure, preventing mass violence by the government in Tahir Square (in contrast to the lack of live coverage and more-widespread violence in Libya).<sup id="cite_ref-391" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-391">[391]</a></sup> The ability of protesters to focus their demonstrations on a single area (with live coverage) was fundamental in Egypt but impossible in Libya, Bahrain and Syria, irrespective of social-media use. A social-media expert launched a network of messages with the hashtag #jan25 on 11 February 2011, when Mubarak’s resignation was announced.<sup id="cite_ref-392" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-392">[392]</a></sup></p>
<p>Journalism scholar <a href="/wiki/Heather_Ford" title="Heather Ford">Heather Ford</a> studied the use of <a href="/wiki/Infoboxes" class="mw-redirect" title="Infoboxes">infoboxes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cleanup_templates" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Cleanup templates">cleanup templates</a> in the Wikipedia article regarding the revolution. Ford claims that infoboxes and cleanup tags were used as objects of "bespoken-code" by Wikipedia editors. By using these elements, editors shaped the news narrative in the first 18 days of the revolution. Ford used the discussion page and the history of edits to the page. She shows how political cartoons were removed, and how the number of casualties became a source of heated debate. Her research exemplifies how editors coordinated and prioritized work on the article, but also how political events are represented through collaborative journalism.<sup id="cite_ref-393" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-393">[393]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-revolutionary_art"><span id="Egyptian_Arts:_Graffiti_.26_Music"></span>Post-revolutionary art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Post-revolutionary art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_Art_in_Egypt#Egyptian_Arts_post_2011_Revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Contemporary Art in Egypt">Egyptian art after the 2011 Revolution</a></div>
<p>The 25 January Revolution and the fall of Hosni Mubarak the following month ushered in a new artistic era reflecting a changed social and political environment;<sup id="cite_ref-394" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-394">[394]</a></sup> "the revolution triggered a new public culture".<sup id="cite_ref-395" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-395">[395]</a></sup> Since its beginning, artists played a significant role in the protests; <a href="/wiki/Street_art" title="Street art">street art</a> and music (<i>electro</i> or <i>techno sha'bi</i>) were used to craft a public culture.<sup id="cite_ref-396" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-396">[396]</a></sup> Artists documented and captured the essence of the revolution, distributing their art through online and face-to-face social networks.<sup id="cite_ref-397" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-397">[397]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also"><span id="Political_activists"></span>See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Egyptian_revolution_of_2011&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">

<dt>General</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20121104170631/https://www.google.com/crisisresponse/egypt.html">Egypt Resources</a> from <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> Crisis Response</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://botw.org/top//Regional/Africa/Egypt/Society_and_Culture/Politics/Protests_2011/">Egyptian revolution of 2011</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Best_of_the_Web_Directory" title="Best of the Web Directory">Best of the Web Directory</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://iamjan25.com/">Media library</a> documenting Egypt's 25 Jan revolution with thousands of videos &amp; photos</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://digitalcollections.aucegypt.edu/">Digital Library</a> includes photos, videos, visual art, and oral histories contributed by student activists, academics, security officers, and demonstrators in and around Cairo.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://archive-it.org/collections/2358">Web Archive</a> includes archived versions of blogs, Twitter feeds, local and regional media coverage, and other sites related to the 25 January Revolution.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.washingtonpost.com/wp-srv/special/world/middle-east-protests/">Middle East and North Africa in turmoil – Tracking the Protests</a>. Chart provided by the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Post" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington Post">Washington Post</a> to keep up day by day with all of the anti-government protests which as off May 2011 are spreading rapidly through the Middle East and North Africa.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.washingtonpost.com/wp-srv/world/special/egypt-transition-timeline/index.html">Timeline: Transition in Egypt</a>. Key events leading up to the first presidential election since the ouster of President Hosni Mubarak and subsequent developments as provided by the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Post" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington Post">Washington Post</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20130525052917/http://www.huffingtonpost.com/2012/06/24/egypt-uprising-election-timeline_n_1622773.html?ref=topbar">Egypt Elections: Key Events Timeline In Egyptian Uprising And Transition</a> as provided by <a href="/wiki/Agence_France-Presse" title="Agence France-Presse">Agence France-Presse</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-middle-east-12327995">Egypt's revolution: Interactive map</a> as provided by <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> News Middle East</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.tahrirarchives.com/">Vox Populi: ongoing project by Lara Baladi which includes a series of media initiatives, art projects, publications and a portal into web based archives</a></li>
</ul>
